O, my sons, since I have now completed this book, which is the last of the sciences of medicine, by fulfilling its purpose and through its explanations and clarifications, it seems to me that I have made clear to you the treatise on what I love most, namely surgery.
For surgery has been greatly neglected in our region and in our time, so that the science of it is almost lost, and its practice has ceased; and there remain only small descriptions of it in the books of the Ancients, which have led to many errors and hesitations because they misrepresent its intentions and weaken its force and art.
And it seems to me that I should revive this treatise according to an order of exposition and explanation and abbreviation; and so that the forms of cautery tools and other instruments used in the work may be clear through addition of explanation and preparation, which is necessary.
But the reason why a good craftsman is not found in this field in our time is that the art of medicine is extensive, and one must be well-versed before being exercised in the science of anatomy, which requires knowledge of the functions of the members and their forms, as well as their connections and separations; and an understanding of the bones, nerves, and ligaments, along with their numbers and arrangements; and of the pulsating veins and static veins, and the places for cutting them.
And for this reason Hippocrates said that one may have the name but little in practice, especially in the art of the hand.
This we have already said at the beginning of this book; for whoever is not versed in what we have said about anatomy will inevitably fall into error by which men are harmed, as I have seen too many of them, for some are trained in this science and yet act without that knowledge and experience.
For I saw a mad physician who had incised an abscess in the neck of a woman and cut into some arteries there so that much blood flowed out, whereupon the woman died almost immediately.
And I saw another physician who claimed that he would remove a stone from a man who was suffering from kidney stones, and the stone was large. When he came to perform the procedure, he removed not only the stone but also part of the bladder, whereupon that man died on the third day.
And I was called to remove that stone and saw that because of the size of the stone and the condition of the patient, I recognized that he would die.
And I saw another physician who received payment from a duke of our land to perform surgery on a black castrated man who had a fracture in his chest. Because of the doctor's ignorance, he exposed the fracture over the wound with padding and bandages in a strong construction but did not allow proper ventilation for the wound.
And he loosened it according to his desires, and then he bandaged it again, and after some days commanded that we remove the bandage. And thus it remained until the chest and ribs became abscessed, leading to his ruin.
And I was called to him, and I prepared to remove the bandage, and upon doing so, he found relief from his pains.
However, if the corruption had already taken hold in the limb, so that it could not be restrained, and the corruption continued to spread throughout the limb until the patient was in danger.
And I saw another physician who incised a cancerous abscess, and it became ulcerated near the thighs, until the malignancy spread throughout much of his lord's body.
The cause of this is that the cancerous condition is pure of melancholic humor, therefore it does not need to affect all parts equally, but rather one limb which can serve as a host for it.
And for this reason, my children, it is necessary for you that the operation be devised in two aspects: one is an operation associated with health, and the other is a procedure in which there is risk even under the best circumstances.
And I have already mentioned this in every place in this book where an operation involves doubt and fear; therefore, it is necessary for you to heed this and avoid that, so that your patients do not find a way to speak ill of you and thus dishonor you.
And so take up your weapons with diligence and the protection of God, and approach your patients with skill and firmness.
And use the best method by the grace of God to lead your patients to health and praiseworthy success, and avoid those terrible diseases that are difficult to heal, and keep at bay what causes you fear, so that hesitation in your faith and reputation does not betray you: for more remains for your glory, and less in this world and ultimately for your sanctity.
Wound treatment in some monastic surgeries: Do not treat illness badly, for then bad doctors are not named.
And thus I have dedicated this book in three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter is ordered about the head, in the forms of instruments and in the tools of cauteries, and of what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, about cutting and puncturing, and about suction cups, and about plagues, and about the extraction of arrows and similar objects, fully chaptered and ordered regarding the forms of instruments.
The third chapter, about restoration and dislocation, and the treatment of twists and similar conditions; a chapter ordered about the head, and regarding the forms of instruments.
I shall speak of the quality of the actions of that cautery and of its ingredients, and in what condition cautery is prohibited.
Therefore, I say that the speech about the quality of the action of cautery and its ingredient is long and verbose, subtle by science, and hidden as a secret; and many wise men have spoken much about it, and they are discordant about it, but for me, a little of their speeches suffices, fearing lengthiness.
And so I say that cauteries confer to all completeness universally, which is of material or without material, except for two completenesses, which are tail completenesses without material.
In the tail and dry completeness of material, they become dried out; for some say that cauteries are helpful for that, and others say the opposite of that, that cauteries are not appropriate in a disease which is by quality and dryness, because the nature of fire is quality and dryness, and it is inappropriate that a tail and dry disease with that which is hot and dry be healed.
And let those who say the opposite of that hear this: that by cauteries help is made in every tail and dry disease which comes to the bodies of men, for when you compare the body of man and the humidity of it to the completeness of fire, you find the body of man frail.
And I say by the speech of that person, that experience has already shown this to me on several occasions.
But I do not intend that it should come to that for one who has already been tested and exercised in the chapter of cauteries by previous exercise, and has known the diversity of human constitution and the disposition of diseases in their seasons, and the causes of those, and their accidents, and the period of time for them.
But in the other completenesses, there is no fear for you, especially in fragile and humid diseases, for all doctors have already agreed on those, and do not disagree about the use of cauteries in them.
And you know, children, that of the secret of healing by cauteries with fire and its goodness is that fire is simple, whose action reaches only the member which is being cauterized and does no harm to other members unless they are adjacent, but slightly affected.
Here is the English translation:
Keep the fire, by its sublimity and by the goodness of its substance, does nothing but superfluify. And it is known to us through experiment and through the length of service in studying the art about the truth of the causes; and thereby I excuse myself from the length of speech. And if not that it were read in my book at this pause would I hide from you secrets about the fire, and the quality of its operation on bodies and of its expulsion for which it expels diseases, with a philosophical demonstrative speech, which would be acute in your ears.
And know, children, that they who neglect the time in which it is convenient to perform cautery miss the best time of spring.
And I say that cautery is convenient at all times, for the harm caused by accidental circumstances of time is submerged in the benefit brought about by what is attracted through cautery, and especially if the cautery is intended to treat severe or necessary pains, which do not tolerate delay because it is feared that such treatments might cause mischief, which is worse than a small harm entering due to the timing.
And this does not concern your ears, or, children, what is valued only by the common people and foolish physicians is that for the illness which is cured by cautery, there is no immediate remedy at all times, and they miss that consequence.
And the reason is not as such that they concern themselves with this, because cautery is merely a substitute for medicine that alters the complexion and dries up the humors which are the cause of pains.
But still, cautery is superior to medicine due to the speed of its effectiveness and the virtue of its operation and the greatness of its power.
It is possible that his illness may return at some time according to the condition of the patient and the location of his illness, and its nature, and what has been prepared in his body by the accumulation of superfluities in him, and what has reached him through their acquisition from food and similar causes; but if the illness for which cauterization is administered is a subtle one involving few superfluities and humidities, such as cauterization of a tooth for pain and the like, it may not return, but this applies to minor cases.
And the common saying is that cauterization is the last remedy of medicine, and this is the barber-surgeon's saying, not what those who understand it say, for I believe that there is no cure that benefits either medicine or any other cause after cauterization.
And the cause is contrary to that; and the understanding that cauterization is the last remedy of medicine is only when we administer methods of treatment for certain illnesses, and those medicines are ineffective, and then at the end of the process cauterization is administered, and it proves effective in such cases. And at those times it was said that cauterization is the last remedy of medicine, not according to the understanding which I understand of the common and excessive practices of foolish doctors.
And the ancients said that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron, and they did not say this except for the equality of gold and the excellence of its substance.
And they said that the location for cauterization does not matter, which is absolutely not true, for I have seen this proven and corrected that it does not work in some bodies as with others, and cauterization with that material is good and better than with iron, just as they say; but when you perform cauterization with fire using gold, it is not clearly defined to you, since it depends on the quantity you want due to the hardness of gold, and how it prepares to receive friction; and if you adjust over it by fire, the gold will melt from the heat, and if you place some sheets in the fire for the craftsman to catch onto during that process.
And thus with water is made the cautery of iron, which should be applied lightly and gradually to the straightness of the operation.
And I have already arranged this chapter on cauteries according to the chapters, and I have arranged those for the head among the weights, so that I alleviate the seeker who asks for what they want.
The first chapter.
This cautery is applied to the cold and humidity that causes a serous accidental condition of which these two causes are responsible: one cause is soda, and there is much rheumatic flux called corsica from the head to the parts of the arms and ears, as well as excessive sleep, toothache, and throat pain, and generally all diseases that come from cold, such as paralysis, stroke, and similar ailments.
The form of this cautery is as follows: first, command the patient to evacuate with laxative medicine for three or four nights, according to the strength of the patient and their age and habit.
And then command him to shave his head with a razor, and then place him seated between two cushions on a tilted bench and have him rest his head on his hands.
And then place the palm of your hand near the nostrils on the shaved part of the nose between the eyes. But when the heat of your middle finger reaches that place, mark that location with ink, and then apply the olive cautery, whose form is as follows:
And apply thereupon that upon the marked spot with a probe, in such a way that it descends, by which the skin may be gradually stretched, and you turn it.
And lift your hand gently and observe the spot, and if you see any quantity of the head bone uncovered, as much as the quantity of a grain of corn, then raise your hand, and if not return your hand to that tool, another of those, if it is chilled, until you see the bone which I have told you about.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it on the spot and let it be for three days.
And then apply on the wound enclosed in a border and let it be on until the burn scab is destroyed. And then treat the spot with a quadruple ointment until it is healed.
And it is said that when the plague wound opens up, the sooner you remove the dressing, the better and more helpful it is.
And some of them say that the wound should be cauterized down to the bone, and the cautery should be applied until it adheres to some part of the bone.
And then remove what is adhered to the bone, and then treat it.
And others say that with the cautery reach to the deepest part, until it presses firmly on the bone until the bone melts, and this is the method of alpechini (scraping off in small bits): And they believe that vapor from the head is expelled from that place.
And let the wound be treated for a long time until it is healed and has become firm.
But I have not seen these two species of cautery used on everyone in all cases, but rather only occasionally out of fear; and let that one be left alone, as far as I am concerned, for it is better to preserve health when possible; because the brain is easily injured by a disruption of continuity as we have testified with other members of the body, and especially when the constitution of the patient is weak.
And the first kind of cautery is safer and better in my opinion; and with that one you can work more safely for health.
Chapter Second.
Of cautery of the head.
When there is chronic and prolonged pain throughout the head, and this condition persists in the patient despite using plasters, cloths, poultices, ointments, and various other treatments, and especially if it has already been treated with a cautery that we have described but without any improvement from those causes, then consider whether the constitution of the patient's head is strong and not weak, and if there is no great fever, at this point apply another form of cautery below that one slightly. And then cauterize that area above the crown of their head with a cautery until the pain is relieved. Cut into the area of pain and expose the bone in the amount that we have described, and cauterize it with a cautery at the back of the head in the place called "vas," which is the location where the vein is situated. And beware in this case, and do not expose the bone, for the patient from this finds great pain different from the pain of all other cauteries of the head.
And I will describe this cautery in its place, for it is more subtle than the one with which you cauterize the horns of the head and the occiput; and this is the form of it.
Chapter Three.
On Cauterization of Non-Ancient Boils.
When severe pain occurs in the part of the head, or if there is lingering pain within the skull, it should be evacuated with medicines that cleanse the head, and the other treatment which I have mentioned for diseases should be administered; and if this does not benefit, then cauterization is done in two ways: either with a sharp burning medicine, or with iron.
But if you use a burning medicine for cauterization, then take a piece of amber and burn it, removing the ends from both sides. And then you should incise the area of the pain on the temple with a lancet lightly so that it is done just below the skin, making it hidden.
And then apply pressure to it with bandages firmly for about three hours; and then loosen them and remove the amber, and let the wound be treated for two or three days, and then place a poultice over it soaked in butter until the area becomes warm.
And then cure that with an ointment until it is healed.
And if you want, use with some medicines burning which I have written in the twelfth chapter of medicines burning.
And the cautery should be made according to this method: fire for the cautery, of which this is the form:
and it is called a nail clamp, because its head is shaped like the form of a nail, in which there is some curvature, and in the middle part of it there is a small prominence.
And then apply it to the place of the pain, and hold your hand — so that the artery below is not adhered to — and turn the iron slightly little by little with speed.
And if the amount of it that adheres by the thickness of the tissue is thus as much as half of it, raise your hand so that the artery below is not adhered to, so that there is no flow of blood.
And then soak cotton in salt water and place that on the site, and leave it for three days; and then apply a poultice of butter, and then treat with an ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize at the end of the radius according to the cautery method, and guard against cutting the artery in this proper but non-traditional procedure.
Chapter four.
On the cautery of an ancient ulcer.
When you treat an ulcer with what we have said about the previous treatment and what we have mentioned in the divisions of diseases, and if it does not benefit from the treatment, and sometimes due to the disease, the initial cautery with medicine or fire is insufficient; then you should ignite the radial cautery until it becomes white-hot. Place it on the site of the pain along a line of length half the breadth of your finger or slightly more, and then hold your hand steady and press it firmly so as not to cut the artery and penetrate to the bone.
However, you must guard against the continuation of the jawbone, which is related to chewing, and ensure that the muscle is not adhered and the nerves which move it are intact, so that no spasm occurs.
And if it is about the care and caution of the flow of blood from the artery that is cut, because there is fear in doing so, especially for one who does not know what they are doing and is not skilled and experienced; and leave the operation to someone at the beginning.
And here comes a reminder of the management of blood flow from an artery according to the usual method, in its place within this book.
And if you see that this illness does not respond to these cauteries and the patient's condition is tolerable, cauterize in the appropriate place of the head as we have described, and treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you do, administer the cautery that we have mentioned in the chapter on the rupture of an artery using a cautery with two edges, and this is a better form of cautery and more effective.
Chapter 5.
On the cautery for ear pains.
When there is pain in the ear due to inflammation and it is treated with laxatives and other treatments as I have described in the division, and if the pain does not improve, now use cautery. This is one that is called puncta, and here is its form:
And apply the puncta near the heat source, and move it around the entire ear as if a wheel is turning, or around each part of the ear if the pain is in that specific area. Lengthen the cautery of the earlobe little by little until you have marked the place with it.
And if you apply the cautery at those points all around the earlobe, and wherever it does that, then take care of the places inside until they heal.
Chapter VI.
On the cautery of the tortula.
The tortula, which is cured by cautery and without it, is due to phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of diseases.
Avoid cautery for the type that results from dryness or spasm of the nerve.
When you treat that type of tortula with jars and capipurgi and gargle remedies, and if your treatment does not comfort the patient, then it is necessary for you to cauterize the disease in three stages: the first at the edge of the earlobe, and the second a little further back near the temple, and the third at the junction of the two lips [likely referring to parts of the ear or affected area].
And pause your cautery at the opposite part of the diseased area, for softening occurs only in the part that appears healthy.
The method of cautery is that you cauterize it with the cautery on the right side of the extremity of the earlobe a little above the horn of the head, and in the temple area another one, whose length should be according to the length of the middle finger, and then pause your hand with the cautery until half the quantity of the thickness is burned.
And this is the form of cautery, and it is specific to the scissors, the form of which is laid out above. However, what is more subtle is that there should be just a little bit as you see; and it is necessary to use the knife instead of excessive coarseness, just a little.
And immediately treat the area with what was previously prepared until it is healed.
Chapter VII.
On the cautery for chronic apoplexy.
When apoplexy is chronic and you treat it with what we have mentioned and your treatment does not induce fever in the patient, then at this time cauterize it with four cauteries: on top of all the temples of the head; and at a cauterium and the middle part of the head, as we have said; and at a cauterium in the nape of the neck. And the method of the cautery is done as was said above.
And it has already been cauterized over the mouth of the stomach; and this is better; and now treat it with what you have prepared.
Chapter VIII.
On the cautery for forgetfulness performed with flame.
It is necessary to give the patient large doses at the beginning and pills that purify the mind.
And then shave the patient's head completely and apply a plaster to the back of the neck, as described in the chapter on plasters.
In some cases, leave some areas untreated, for that is the method of cauterization. Do this according to the order which I have already mentioned.
And if it heals that way, it is well; but if not, then at this time apply three cauterizations to the rear part of his head, which should be performed from the most prominent part of the head down towards the neck. And wait between each cauterization for the size of a pea, and then treat the cauterized area with what has been described.
But if you prefer and the condition allows it, then apply the cauterization in the middle location; and if you want a stronger effect, then cauterize above both temples.
Then treat it so that it may heal; and let the cauterizations be olive-shaped as described before.
Chapter IX.
On the cauterization of parasites and the softening of the entire body.
Proceed with the cleansing of the head using scissors and as we have discussed; then shave the patient's head, and afterwards cauterize it in the middle part of the head, and on top of all the temples, and on the rear part of the head, and with three cauterizations on the vertebrae of the neck.
And if it is necessary for the illness of softening the body to do so and the patient can tolerate it and the illness is severe, then cauterize with four cauterizations on the vertebrae of the back, and finally apply one cauterization in a place that will be consumed by the condition, and raise the heat gradually. For one inside which is to be healed, use this; for the other which is to be cauterized, use olive.
Chapter 10.
On the cautery for epilepsy.
An epileptic is not cauterized unless the epilepsy is due to phlegm.
And therefore, it is necessary to modify the brain of the patient at the beginning of the year and use the other treatment that we have described in the division when the patient is strong and can tolerate the exception of medicines.
And if it is a child who cannot tolerate medicines, then administer sweets and chewables that purify the brain, for several days beforehand, along with the improvement of diet.
And then shave the patient's head, and afterwards cauterize one spot in the middle of the head according to the previous method, and with another cauterization at the nape of the neck, and on each side of the head cauterize as well. And if the patient is strong and can tolerate it, then cauterize as I have described, with paralysis and relaxation of the body over the cervical vertebrae and the thoracic vertebrae.
And use olive for cauterization as was previously mentioned.
But if the patient is a child, then use a gentle cauterization according to this method.
Chapter 11.
On the cautery for melancholia.
When the causes of melancholia are corrupted humidity and thick phlegm, then one should cauterize that area with the cauteries which we have mentioned in the case of paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholia is excess declining to melancholia, and the body of the patient is humid, then one should give him to drink what purifies his brain, as mentioned above according to what has been discussed in the division.
And then shave the patient's head, and then make a small incision carefully around the circumference, and then place that in the middle of the head.
And let the patient be seated straight, and hold him from all sides.
And then apply a liniment; it is more suitable to use something like lamb's wool. And heat that on the fire with moderate warming according to measure, which the patient endures when it is applied. And then place that in the middle of the patient's head in a circle, and let it be rubbed inside so that it is applied.
And do this for the patient once a week according to his good condition until he is healed; or if you prefer, cauterize those points as needed based on what is best for him, but hold your hand with a cautery arranged in such an order: this is a special kind of cautery that moistens the brain with equilibrium.
And then place that paste wrapped in lamb's wool or linen cloth.
Chapter XII.
Of cautery for water descending from the bladder.
When you have declared the beginning of water in the urine by the signs which I have mentioned in the division, then give the patient to drink water that purifies the head [brain], and keep him from all humidity.
And make him sweat in warm baths for several days.
And then command him to shave his head, and cauterize that part where the cauterization is in the middle of the head; and afterward cauterize that part with two cauteries on both temples if it is the beginning of water descending into two bladders, or from one side if it is the beginning of water in one bladder.
Incise the veins and arteries that you see along with the cauteries; and let the incisions be at a distance in the latitude of the temples.
And guard against bleeding when he is being cauterized; and if you see him bleed, stop the bleeding with some medicine that is available to you.
And I will come to the knowledge that is in the extraction of arteries and the treatment to prevent bleeding.
And in some cases, cauterize the neck between two bones with two cauteries placed backwards.
Chapter XIII.
Of the cautery for old tears.
When tears from the eyes are old and constant, passing through veins and arteries that are in a visible part of the head, and it is verified that this is due to cold phlegmatic excess, then cauterize at the point where we have described in the chapter on the beginning of water descending into the bladder, with one cauterization in the middle of the head, and two cauteries on both temples, and two cauteries on the neck between two bones. And if you need an addition, then cauterize that at the root of the nose on the extremity of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter xiiii.
On Cauterization for Nasal Odor.
When you treat that person with what we have said in the division and they do not benefit from your treatment, then prepare and give the sick person decoctions to drink for three nights; afterwards, shave their head, and cauterize them with a medium-sized olive cautery. And then cauterize that with a carnation-sized cautery on both eyebrows above the hair, slightly above.
And be careful with the cauterization so that you do not burn it excessively.
And this is the form of the carnation-sized cautery.
Chapter .xv.
On Cauterization for Softening the Eyelids.
When the eyelid is softened due to illness or moisture, then cauterize that eyelid with this lunar-shaped cautery; and this is the form of it:
And if you do so, cauterize that above both eyebrows slightly with two cauteries in total, and let them be longer toward the temples. And let the length of each cautery be according to the quantity of the eyebrow.
And do not press too hard with your hand on the cautery, but rather so that one-third of the eyelid adheres.
Chapter XVI.
About cauterization of the eyelid when the hair inside turns white and falls out.
When the hair of the eyelid turns white and falls out, cauterization should be performed in two ways: either by burning or by using a combustible medicine.
If cauterization is done with fire, instruct the patient to shave the hair around the affected area as people do when getting a haircut, ensuring that it is extended and even; and if the hair falls out from its root, then wrap the eyelids with a bandage so that nothing interferes until new hair grows.
Once the hair grows back and becomes even, then instruct the patient to place their head in a warm environment, and gently apply something on the eyelids where the cauterization took place, in the shape of a leaf of mercury, with the beginning of the application near the hair.
And then apply cotogna pulp in egg white or mucilage of fenugreek on the eyelid.
And thus the cauterization should be performed according to this method.
And then cauterize over the figure you have marked, little by little, in small stages, until the surface of the area shaped like a myrtle leaf is completely burned through.
And a sign of the successful treatment is that you see the eyelid becoming contracted and the hair on it raised from the puncture of the eyelid.
Therefore raise your hand and lay it on that place for three days, and then pause above the wound until the scar has healed. And then treat that hand until it is healed.
If the hair returns for any reason around them and the eyelid is softened, now perform the cautery on that area again as you did initially.
But if the hair is on the lower eyelid, now cauterize that area until it rises to its natural place and heals so that the hair does not obstruct the eye.
But with the cautery and burning medicine, this is what you should order the patient to do: read the hair until it grows long and heals, and then make from cloth a leaf of myrtle shape.
And then take known soap and one ounce of living herbs, and mix those ingredients well, using it quickly so that it is not rubbed; then spread this over the cloth made in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and place it on both eyes; and cover the eye wound with albumen, and have the patient's head in your lap.
And place your finger on the medicine and press it gently so that the patient feels the stinging of the medicine, for this stinging is like that of fire.
And as long as the burning sensation persists, then remove the medicine and wash it with your finger; when the stinging stops, now wash the eye with water; then consider if you see the eyelid raised as it should be, in which case proceed with fire or incision, for this is good; but if not, return to applying the medicine to the place where it has not been applied yet and do not let it darken until your operation is healed and the eye contracts.
Then pause at the cut on the edge until the pain of the injury is eradicated, and then treat that which is found to be infected or otherwise until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you to guard your operation with diligent vigilance so that no part of the medicine falls into the eye. And if the eyelid after some days becomes softened and any cause of the hairs pushes in the eye, then repeat your medicine on that place where the eyelid has become soft, and properly as you did at the beginning, and then around until it is healed.
And this is the form of the mirror leaf.
And you should know that the eyes of people vary in size and smallness, and therefore according to this, your treatment is adjusted.
And the way to practice this art will not be hidden from one who studies in this field.
Chapter XVII.
On the cautery treatment of a fistula in the tear duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula with what we have said in the divisions of diseases and do not confine your treatment, then it is necessary that you cauterize it according to this method: instruct the patient to rest his head on your thigh, and hold his head still between your hands in such a way that it does not move or shake.
And then place a cut piece of egg white or mucilage of starch on the eye.
Here is the fire for cautery, which has this form:
which should be hollow, as is the shape of a reed or the bone of a bird, at one end of which the cautery is made.
And if you wish, it should be penetrable into the other end. And if you want it to be as thin as a needle.
But the one that is concave is better for this work.
And if the fistula is open, press it and let it dry out. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the pus from it.
And then place on it the cautery which should be well heated, and hold it with your hand a little away from the eye, so that you do not miss your mark or cause harm to the eye, and then place the cautery on the eyelid and burn it.
And if at the beginning of your cautery it penetrates into the bone, that is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if necessary; and leave it like that for three days, and then place a dressing around the edge and apply a drying agent around it until it is healed.
But if after these days it has not healed, then apply a sharp corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed and treat it as appropriate according to its chapter.
And there is another method of cautery for a fistula, which some earlier practitioners described as follows: Go to the location of the fistula and open it, then place in that fissure a small amount of melted lead; hold your hand above carefully with good control, so that the eye is not harmed because the melted lead does not reach the eye; and make sure you place over the wound a dressing soaked in egg white or water, for the lead adheres to the location of the fistula and heals it miraculously.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said about cauterization and treatment, that is well; but otherwise, it is necessary to administer trephining of the nose and reduce the fistula to the course of the nose as it will come to its proper place.
Chapter XVIII.
On Cauterization of the Fistula of the Anus.

Chapter XIX.
On Cauterization of Fistula in the Mouth.
When an abscess forms on the uvula or palate or in the gums around the teeth, and it bursts, and then becomes inflamed, and a fistula is formed, and when you treat that fistula and your treatment does not take effect, then it is necessary to prepare a cautery according to the size of the fistula, and apply the heated cautery into the fistula, with your hand until the red-hot cautery reaches the depth of it; do this once or twice; and then treat it with what we have said about treatment until it is healed.
Therefore, if the sinus is excised and it heals, that is good; but otherwise, it is necessary to discover the location and remove the corrupted bone as will be explained in its chapter.
Chapter XX.
On cauterization of the teeth and softening of the gums.
When the gums have been softened by humidity and the teeth are unhealthy, and you treat them with medicines and do not confine them, then place the head of the cautery in your mouth, and quickly ignite it to form as follows: place the tube on the tooth and insert the heated cautery rapidly into it, so that the patient feels the heat from the fire reaching the root of the tooth.
And then remove your hand, and subsequently repeat the cautery several times as you wish.
And then the patient should rinse their mouth with salt water and hold it for one hour, after which they can spit it out, as the unhealthy tooth is treated and the softened gum becomes firm and the corrupted moisture is dried up.
Chapter XXI.
On cauterization of toothache.
When toothache is due to coldness, or there is decay or inflammation, and you do not confine the treatment with medicine, then cauterization in such cases can be done in two ways: either by using a cow's horn or by using fire.
Cauterization with a horn is when you take a cow's horn and boil it in oil, then take cotton and wrap it around the tip of the horn. Subsequently, immerse it in the boiled horn until it is soaked, and quickly place it on the painful tooth to rub it gently.
From there, apply some cautery until the heat of the fire reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you do, submerge wool or cotton in the liquid mentioned above, and place that on the aching tooth, and put on top of it an iron instrument heated until the iron reaches the pulp of the tooth.
However, in the case of cautery with fire, you should wait for the moment when it is made of horn or iron, and it should be in the center of some thickness so that the intensity of the heat does not reach the mouth of the patient.
And then heat the cautery in the form that will come, and place it on the tooth and move it with your hand until the cautery is rubbed; do this for several days until the pain subsides either on that day or the next day.
And it is necessary that by pressing that cautery, the patient fills their mouth with good liquid and holds it for an hour, and then spits it out; this is the form of the procedure.
And cauterize as much as you wish, according to what is possible for you.
And this is the form of the tube:
Chapter XXII.
On the Cauterization of Hemorrhoids.
When hemorrhoids are caused by phlegm and humors that resist digestion despite medication and you wish to digest them lightly, then heat the cautery in the form described below, which has two open ends so that smoke can escape. And if the abscess is small, wait according to the size of the abscess before applying cauterization, and then leave it for three days and place it on a board so that what the fire has burned can reassemble.
And then take care of that which was opened and dress it with bandages until it is healed.

Chapter XXVII.
On the Cauterization of an Inflamed Kidney.
When pain arises in the kidney due to coldness or moisture or excessive windiness causing an abnormal evacuation beyond what is normal, and if the illness is treated as we have said without compression, then the patient should be placed on his back with his head raised, and you should feel with your hand three cauterizations according to this form and quantity, placing them on the kidney below the hypochondria, where a person lies.

Chapter XXVIII.
On the Incision of an Abscess of the Kidney with Cauterization.
When you discover an abscess or swelling in the kidney and wish to know whether that abscess is in the flesh of the kidney or in its capsule, if it is in the flesh of the kidney, the patient will experience heaviness and pain without acute sharpness.
And if it is in the capsule of the kidney, the pain will be intense and often exhaust the efforts of the physicians.
Therefore, have the patient lie on his side with the affected part upwards; then identify the location of the abscess by examining it; and subsequently use the cautery that resembles a reed, the form of which was described on the previous page. And cauterize with that cautery until the entire tube is consumed, until it reaches the bladder, until it causes healthy scabbing.
And then treat that patient as you would for other abscesses until he is healed.
And it is not appropriate to use this form of cautery except by one whose study in the art of medicine is extensive and who has recognized many diseases; but this should be attempted only a few times on such an operation, but rather leave that task to us, as it is better.
Chapter XXIX.
On Cauterization of Fistulas.
Take the old cauterizing materials from the bark of the artemisia for fistulas according to this method. And here is how it should be done: Take a piece of dried artemisia bark that is long and thin, about as thick as your finger.

Then cauterize with a cautery at the place between the clavicle and the neck; and two small cauters below this one, slightly inclined towards the part beneath the beard; and two cauters inclining between the fifth rib and the twelfth, slightly inclined towards the rear parts; and another cautery in the middle of the feet; and one on the stomach; and three at the rear parts; and one at the place between the two shoulders; and two cauterizations at each side of the foot, below the one that is between the two shoulders.
And it is not necessary to cover the wound with dressings at the cauterizations, but rather leave it exposed.
And some ancient authors also say that for some men who use a cautery made of iron resembling a red-hot rod and ignite it, place this in the location between the ribs until it reaches the abscess, where the pus should be evacuated, as we have said regarding the abscess of the testicle.
But in this puncture with the cautery there is no fear, for if it happens that death occurs or an accident happens at the location of the fistula, there is no cure.
Chapter XXX.
On the cautery of the meatus.

And do not press your hand against the cautery; and if the disease is on both sides, treat them in the same way.
And there is another method of cauterizing, which is to ignite the cautery vertically in two places, as we have mentioned in the chapter on dislocation of the muscle, and elevate the shoulder, which is on the side of the meatus where the disease on the left side spreads.
And your elevation should be such that when you raise the shoulder according to the length of the body, the cauterizations are made according to the width of the body.
And then immediately insert the two cauteries, ignite them, and penetrate with them into the space where they can reach the shoulder on the other side.
And then remove the cautery; thus, four cauterizations are completed.
And if you wish to cauterize, have three different kinds of cautery instruments.
And when you treat the cautery, be careful not to move the arm too much for several days, because it is more convenient than other preceding causes in treatment.

Then place the cautery on that mark with your hand holding the cautery straight, and do so until you feel the bone of the phalanx under the skin; and repeat it if you did not succeed in doing so during the first attempt to reach the bone.
Observe carefully the condition of the flap during your cautery, so that you do not burn it, thereby preventing death or other great harm to the patient from occurring due to the wound.
And know that when the cautery does not reach the bone, do not proceed with your operation.
And it is appropriate that the cautery for children be gentle according to their size and measure.
Regarding the care of the area for cauterization, for three days apply something around it so that the fire scar is softened.
And regarding the care with other ointments until it is healed.
And the patient should lie on their back for forty days until the area is sealed and the plague subsides.
And it is necessary to restrict the patient's nutrition according to the length of time required for the healing of their intestines, and ensure that the bowel does not protrude due to excessive effort.
And when it is time to remove the dressing after forty days, administer it properly, and keep that in place for another forty days.
And reduce work, eating, drinking, and speaking loudly, for when one follows this complete regimen of healing, they will be healed.
And I will describe the treatment of ruptures in other parts of the abdomen, and at the beginning, if you do not want them to spread, then cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to its size, and let that be removed as much as one-third of it; and thereafter treat it as we have said, and no further adjustment is needed.

Chapter XLVI.
On cauterizing for dislocation.
When pain occurs in any member due to falling or striking and that pain persists for a long time and is not relieved by medicine or cure, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And the cautery for each member should be according to its size and smallness, and strength and weakness, and the location of the dislocation and pain.
If it is healed by the first cautery, that is good; but if not, then repeat the cautery. For it is customary with such pains that they puncture a member for another member nearby, so you must achieve this with the cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter XLVII.
On cauterizing for leprosy.
Some cases of leprosy are greatly helped by cautery, but rarely when the type of leprosy is caused by the putrefaction of flesh and melancholy.
And therefore, when you wish to cauterize that patient, consider whether the leprosy is commensurate and can be cured by what we have mentioned in the division, and do not delay if it is present and you fear that the corruption will encompass the whole body of the patient and all its composition, then cauterize on the head with five cauteries: one at the middle part of the head, and another further down near the forehead; two on the temples; and one behind, on the nape or neck.
And finally, your cautery should be slight inside so that it presses on the bone a little and thereby displaces the scales, so that the exhalation of thick vapors may be alleviated by this; and cauterize over the wound as is necessary.
And if leprosy has already spread over the body and is manifestly apparent, now you must cauterize there with those cauteries which we have mentioned at the beginning; and one cautery on the tip of the nose, and two cauteries on each cheek, and two cauteries on the two vertebrae of the neck, and six cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and one large one near the buttocks, and one on the buttock, and two on the thighs, and one on each thigh, and two on the knees, and two on the muscles, and two on the tendons of their feet; and cauterize there over all the joints of the aforementioned fingers and toes, and over each heel of their feet, and over the creases of their skin.
And be careful of the nerves which run over the heel so that you do not burn them.
And in some cases it is cauterized with one cautery on the bone of the little toe and another on the mouth of the stomach, and another on the liver.
And know that the more you adjust the cauteries, the more beneficial and effective they are; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery as others would, because his body is already numbed.
And it is appropriate that the cauteries be large or small according to the members and joints as is necessary depending on the nature of the tools.
Regarding the treatment with cautery, use flour of wheat mixed with honey. For the other cure, let it be made whole.
Chapter XLVIII.
On cauterization for stupor.
When a limb is numbed or asleep and is treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and does not heal, then you should cauterize that numb limb with cauteries according to the size of the limb or its smallness.
And let the heat of the cautery enter slightly under the skin. And subsequently treat that with an ointment so that it may heal.
And sometimes a numbness is cauterized which extends to the ribs and spine, and along the course of the nerves that control that limb, and the numbness is alleviated.
But do not proceed with this except for one who is knowledgeable about the anatomy of limbs and the course of nerves throughout the moving body.
Chapter XLIX.
On cauterization of paralysis that is caused by palsy.
When paralysis is long-standing and does not respond to any of the medicinal ointments, then you should cauterize it with a cautery slightly, according to the quantity of the skin's thickness, until the whiteness and color change, and its color is altered.
And subsequently treat that with flour of wheat and lentils mixed with rose oil and leaves of arnica, and either pigeon blood or eagle blood, taking a part of each and mixing them all together, then apply it to a cloth and place it over the area until it is healed.
Chapter I.
On cauterizing ulcers.
When cancer begins and you want to remove it, then cauterize that part with a cauterizing instrument in the form of a circle around it as it appears.
And the wise ones say that it should be cauterized with the last cauterization in its proper place; but to me this has not been seen, for I fear that it will become ulcerated, and I have already seen this happen in some cases.
It is more proper to cauterize around the part in a circular manner as we have said, or with the words "cauterize" [likely meaning to follow the previously described method].
Chapter II.
On cauterizing of hemorrhoids.
When hemorrhoids are delayed in maturation due to excess moisture in that area, or due to the patient's condition, being old and weak with little blood, or due to time, and you want to hasten their maturation; then cauterize around them with small punctures using a cauterizing instrument; and immediately let it be so that they will mature more quickly.
But if you want to perforate with the cauterizing instrument, then take the cauterizing tool which has this shape; and place it in the proper location of the hemorrhoid until it penetrates the tissue.
And if the cauterization is done in a location that is further away to spare the arm; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter LII.
On cauterization with corrosives.
A corrosive is a wandering corruption in the limb, which corrodes as fire consumes dry wood.
Therefore, when you see that corrosive in a place where it can endure cautery with fire, then you ignite and cauterize small nails, and larger ones as is appropriate in the place where the corrosion is.
And then cauterize it thoroughly from all sides until you eradicate the entire corrosion and leave no cause for its recurrence.
And then dress it for three days, and apply ointment three times a day on the cauterized areas until all scars are eradicated and all corruption is eliminated.
And then treat it with substances that promote flesh growth.
But if after three days you see that healthy flesh is growing where there is no corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cautery on what remains in the corrupted areas.
And some corrosives are treated with sharp medicines, for those can be used as an alternative to cautery. But cautery with fire is more quickly effective.
And I have already mentioned the treatment with sharp medicines in the division above, and therefore use that when you find it appropriate.
Chapter LIII.
On cauterization of nails and other similar treatments.
In many cases, near the joints of the foot when there is a severe illness that is called "claw," which is caused by swelling and cutting into the foot; and cautery in those cases is done in two ways, namely hot fire cautery and hot water cautery.
The hot fire cautery is made by igniting the cautery in the shape of a ring, which is made of iron according to the quantity that contains the claw from all sides, and it should be thin.
And then place the ignited cautery on the claw, and then move your hand with the cautery around the circumference of the claw until the cautery reaches the depth of the claw. And leave that for three days until it begins to form scabs. And then apply a plaster made of crushed wild mullein and salt to it, and leave the plaster on for one night, for that will eradicate it from its roots.
And then treat the area with a generative flesh ointment until it is healed.
And if not converted, and in many cases it happens on the surface of the body, mainly on the hands and feet, then you should administer hot water cautery to them. Take a small tube made of horn or iron or ring-shaped, and place it on the claw; then pour a little hot water into the tube, hold your hand, and move that with the pressure of your hand completely, and apply the edge of the tube to the root of the claw; and watch as the hot water flows out through the opening at the root of the claw. Wait for a short time after the burning sensation from the hot water for about an hour, then remove it, for the claw with its roots will be eradicated.
And thus place one next to the other until you cover all of them according to their natural position in the body.
And place near the cure those pus which the roots of the nails are eradicated with the help of medicines that cause flesh to grow.
Chapter LIII.
On the cautery for tremor.
When you find a tremor due to irritation in the nerves or from fever causing irritation, or from some similar cause, it is then that you should cauterize it with four cauteries or five on the vertebrae of the back, between each vertebra one caustic; and at the feet of the patient, and above the stomach, apply an olive-shaped caustic.
And the tremor of the patient subsides and the health improves quickly.
Chapter LIV.
On the cautery for accidental injuries to the body.
When in the body there appear accidental swellings and sores that are formed from thick corrupted matter; therefore, at the beginning when they appear, it is necessary to cauterize over the entire head of the fistula with a fine caustic made from myrtle wood, the end of which is extended over fire; or with long eagle claws; or with a lentil-shaped caustic.
And sometimes the caustic is also applied at the beginning of its expulsion according to this method; and if it does not work as expected, the excess is removed, and thus it heals from those issues.
However, it must be done near evacuation with phlebotomy.
Chapter LVI.
On cauterization of blood flow due to accidental cutting of an artery.
Treat quickly the flow of blood from an artery that has been cut due to the disease which caused it beforehand, or due to the beginning of abscesses or cauterization of the limb, and in similar cases, as it is difficult to remedy such situations.
When such an accident happens, immediately place your hand on the opening of the artery, and press over it with firmness until the flow of blood stops below your hand, ensuring it does not continue to bleed.
And then place in the fire olive coals, both small and large, for many moments, and blow on them until they are ignited.
And then take one of those coals, either small or large depending on the location and disease in which the artery is ruptured, and place it quickly on that vein after you have pressed your hand, so that the cauterization stops and restrains the blood.
And if the blood is expelled from the opening of the artery when you press your hand and remove the cauterization, then take another coal promptly from those coals which are prepared in the fire, and continue to do this one after another until the bleeding is stopped.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is here, and ensure it does not cause other harm.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, it is not possible to restrain the blood at that time, especially if the artery is large, unless one uses four methods: either use cauterization as we have mentioned, or perform an extraction because when it is extracted the extremities of its ends are consumed and thus the blood is restrained, or bind it with a bandage made of strong material, or place it upon medicines whose virtue is to constrict the blood; and secure it with a pad using proper constriction.
And may he who administers proper constriction with bandages and by positioning hot causes and similar methods not hurry but act slowly.
And if one attempts this and the physician is not present nor are medicines available, then gently press with the index finger on the opening of that vein as we have said, and understand the great constriction within so that the blood remains in the upper part of the wound, and do not move the finger over the artery; and sprinkle it above the artery with cold water to make the blood congeal and thicken; and then decide whether cauterization or medicine is necessary.
On incisions and perforations and wind disorders and phlebotomy and wounds and their similar conditions.
We have already discussed in the first chapter all diseases in which cautery with fire is appropriate and with medicines that cause burning, along with their occasions and causes, and the instruments necessary for the forms of cauterization; and I have organized the chapters on the superiority of the head over the body.
In this procedure described in this chapter, may the seeker be praised for it, at the time concerning what they have asked.
And before I begin this, take note, my son, that in this chapter it is by way of what is in the first chapter on cauterization.
And for this reason, it is necessary to be in great caution, because in the operation which is in this chapter many times evacuation of what is harmful to life may occur through the application of venesection or incision over an abscess or opening of an exit or cleansing of a plague or extraction of a shard or beginning of stone removal and similar procedures. And with all of these procedures, accompany fear and trembling, and in the most severe cases, death may result.
And I warn you that this does not fall upon you in the form of retribution, for at times your actions may affect many people suffering from various illnesses. And among them are those who become angry about their illness and die quickly because of the intensity of what they find in their illness, and due to the length of their suffering, and the illness itself brings fear which signifies death.
And among them is one who gives you all their possessions and says, "I trust in you for my health," while their illness is deadly.
And therefore, you should not consent to those who come to you in this manner; and your caution should be greater than your greed; and do not assume any cause unless it is confirmed by certain knowledge that verifies what concerns you, and proceed with praiseworthy succession.
For the treatment of all your patients, make a prognosis and indication of when it is to their health converted, for in that you will have aid toward the acquisition of fame and of memory, of praise and of glory.
O, my children, may God inspire your understanding and not deprive you of direction and protection, for these are in His hands.
And I have ordered this chapter to be distinguished by sections as it follows the chapter on cauterization, concerning the treatment of the head among other parts, so that what you seek and need from it may bring you joy.
First Chapter.
On the treatment of water that is contained in the head of children.
Know that this illness often afflicts children shortly after birth and when the nurse presses the child’s head too vigorously.
And sometimes it happens for hidden causes that are not known.
But I have seen this illness only in children; and for all those whom I treated, death followed, and thus it seemed to me that there was no remedy for them.
And I saw a child whose head was filled with water, and his head was so enlarged that he could not control himself because of the size of his head, and before long the moisture increased to such an extent that it caused death.
And this moisture may be aggregated between the membrane and the bone, or below the bone on the cerebrum.
For the operation in that case where the humidity is between the cartilage and the bone and the abscess is small, you should cut into the middle part of the head with a cleft in its length, and let this incision be about two nights long where the moisture runs, and this is the form of the spatum:
And if the humidity is more increased and the abscess is larger, then there are two clefts made in this manner:
And if the humidity is deeper than the bone, the sign of this is that you see the seams of the head covered all around and the water is squeezed when you press it with your hand on the surface but not from within. You should cut into the middle part of the head with three clefts in this manner:
and immediately the incision extracts all the moisture; and then cover the clefts with bandages and plasters; and afterwards apply ointment made of oil and wine over it from the fifth day, and then loosen the binding and treat the wound with lint and herbs, and bind the head tightly; and eat light dried food with little moisture until the member is strengthened and healed.
And there is another method of incision, which you should consider based on the size of the abscess and the collection of water, for sometimes it is more in the occipital region, or in the front part, or on the right side, or on the left; and therefore pay attention to the incision where the abscess appears and the swelling of the water, and do this as best suits you.
And observe that you do not cut the artery and beware of excessive bleeding, for the illness may worsen due to that loss of blood rather than from the evacuation of moisture.
Chapter Second.
When I come to certain cases of hemorrhoids or piles that are old and have not been treated with medicines, then the last resort is to treat them by cutting into those two arteries.
And therefore, when you wish to incise those arteries, have the patient lay his head down and rub the area with a rough cloth so that the veins appear; then secure the patient's neck with a cloth. Then feel for where the vein is pulsing; these locations are near the ears, though they may be somewhat hidden in some people.
And then press on the artery with your finger, and then incise each one with a sharp scalpel, making the incision deep enough to reach the bone, and this should be done according to the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the scalpel below the artery and cut it at the points where the incisions are above.
And the length of the incision should be about two fingers wide, for when an artery is cut, blood flows out in a pulsating manner and spurts frequently.
But if the artery does not appear clearly, then measure from the ear a distance of three fingers, press with your finger there, and make an incision into the bone.
In what one should use to clean the wound is two ounces of vinegar, and it is light to pour from barley or wheat flour, and all this is according to the quantity which appears of the strength of the patient and the fullness of the artery.
And then consider the condition, and if any cause of suppuration remains on the bone, now incise that so it does not become an abscess from cauterization.
And then strengthen the wound with poultices and bandages of cloth, and afterwards care for it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter III.
Of the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
When it happens that someone suffers from a sudden illness or acute fever due to excessive humidity in the stomach and its quality reflected in the temples, or if it is an old disease with similar symptoms, and it does not respond to treatment methods of medicine, then we resort to these illnesses the extraction of arteries in the temples, or cauterization of those as we have shown.
And the method of the operation and extraction of those is that the patient shaves the hair which is in the temples; and understand the artery that appears on the temple as it is shown to you by its pulse, and it is hidden only in a few people due to the intensity of sweating.
And if it is hidden from you, then tie the patient's head with some cloth; and afterwards rub the area with a rough cloth or evaporate the area with hot water until the artery becomes clearly visible. And then you take the spatula which has this shape: And then you peel the gland so that it is easily entered to reach the artery; and then cut around it until you extract it from the gland and reveal those below it, from all sides.
But if the artery is fine, then wrap it at its extremity with a ligature, and then cut a part of it according to the amount by which the extremities are lengthened and contracted, and there will be no flow of blood, for if not done, the blood will not rise at all.
And then evacuate the blood by about one ounce up to three ounces.
And if the artery is large, then you should tie it in two places with a double thread of fine silk or strong cords, and do not allow it to putrefy before the consolidation of the wound and thus prevent the flow of blood.
And then cut away the excess that is between the two ligatures; do this at that hour or another time.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery to the bone or use a knife-shaped cautery until the extremities are incised, and this will serve as a substitute for that procedure.
And it is better to do this when the patient does not have a fever or is of heated complexion; for cautery is among those causes that help eliminate final moisture, and thus it is better at the beginning.
And after the extraction of the arteries, fill the place with dried meat, and place it on powder prepared properly, and then treat it with dry medicines that promote flesh growth and dressings until it is healed.
And if while you are operating elsewhere there comes a flow of blood from the artery, then you should proceed to restrain that blood, either by cauterizing or by filling the place with cotton, and strengthen your hand so that the blood is constricted. And if none of those causes is present, then pause your hand on the place until the blood clots, and apply the area with water of extreme coldness until the pain subsides, and it becomes strengthened as needed.
And what is more urgent in the extraction of an artery is that you cauterize the vein with this cautery having two knives, after you have marked both places with ink, and then apply some coals, on fire, until they reach the bone and cut the vein into two places, so that it can be extended between its extremities, because they are not fully consolidated by this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And the sharpness of the two knives should be exemplary. However, if you have less sharp knives than two, it is better, for if your knives are as sharp as two knives, approach each cut slowly and do not cut the flesh too quickly.
Therefore, when there is any swelling in those areas, keep the heat of the fire on them and cut the flesh quickly.
And this work is better than all other works and faster.
And the space between the two knives should be the width of a finger.
Chapter four.
On the treatment of sharp and acidic tears of the eyes.
When such acidic tears occur, it is because of the condition of those veins which are above the head on the forehead, and if no cause for treatment with medicine is found there, then the face of the patient may become red, and they feel a creeping sensation in their forehead as if ants were crawling, and the eyes from this so weakened, become moist to such an extent that even the hairs around them can be seen, and the eyelids are scorched by the sharpness of the tears. The ancient surgeons used bitter mixtures for treating these conditions, and they would perform this treatment without delay.
And it is that you command the patient to pluck out the hairs that are on their forehead, and then make three incisions in the forehead equally spaced according to the length of the forehead. And if the length of the incision is two fingers: one incision should be equally distant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length a little ahead of where the tears flow from, and the third on the other side.
And be careful of the beginning of the arteries that are on both sides, and extend your hand along the continuation of both jaws. And if the space between each incision is three fingers wide, adjust it accordingly.
And if you have a piece of prepared sponge or dry cloth with which to absorb the blood.
And then insert the blade of two sharp points, which has this form for the incision: which is of the part dealing with plates into the second inscription. Cut out from that all the chain which is between two incisions at the suture which is on the bone, and then do this for the inscription by going into the second incision. And then split in this place and insert into the first incision another tool called a knife, sharp on one side and not sharp on the other.
And place the sharp edge towards the flesh adjacent to the chain, and the blunt edge inside the bone, and insert it until it reaches the middle fissure, and cut with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the heels.
But continue the incision where it appears from the chain, and then do this in the middle fissure into the other fissure.
And when the blood flows in equal quantity, press the places where the clotted blood is located.
And then place in each fissure dry wool soaked in vinegar and oil mixed with sweet wine, and apply it so that no abscess forms.
And on the third day remove the bandage and administer generous infusions of hot water or decoction, and then treat it with basilicon in rose oil and other remedies until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 5.
On the cure of ulcers by catharsis and for those descending from parts inside the head.
When it comes to some veins that are sharp and continuous, and the patient's eyes are weakened and small, and often their care is debilitated, and the eyelids are ulcerated and from this case the hair falls out, and in the forehead there is a sharp pain with subsequent exhaustion, then you should know by these accidents that the matter and cathartics do not come but from veins of the inner parts proceeding.
And the best cure in those cases is this: that you order the patient to shave their forehead, and then cut a small incision at the middle of the forehead (beginning from the temple on the left towards the right temple; and make the incision into the bone; extend your hand beyond the temples which are near chewing), until the bone is exposed.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or a dry cloth and then fill between the two lips of the incision with hot cheese or with cloths. And then cover above with powdered herbs soaked in wine and oil so that it does not result in an infected sore.
And when you loosen this and see that the abscess has already diminished, now cover the bone where it begins with new flesh, and then cure it with a drying regimen which makes new flesh grow, as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony and mix them together, and from these make a plaster to be applied to the wounds until new flesh grows.
Chapter VI.
On the treatment of what affects the ears.
The cause that lies in the ears is one of four kinds: either it is a mineral stone or something like a stone, such as iron or lead; or it is from a plant, such as thorns or beans; or it is fluid-like, such as water and urine and things similar to them; or some animal.
And therefore when a stone or any other cause similar to those causes that do not adhere nor fuse in the ear enters the ear, one should expose the ear to the sun and if it moves the stone, then at this time distill some cause of violet oil or sesame oil into it, and immediately administer its extraction with condiments, and make them sneeze by means of this occurrence. And then place around the ear some pieces of cloth or wool, and stretch the ear towards the upper parts, as it often happens that these causes are expelled by such actions.
And if it is not expelled, then administer the extraction with an instrument called a gentle epicycle, which has this form:
And if it is expelled by the instrument, that is good; and if not, then administer the extraction with a small amount of powdered substance.
And if it is not expelled by these methods, then make a tube of reed and insert the tip of the tube into the ear canal, and secure around the tube with wax and softened oil so that nothing else enters except through the tube. And then extract with gentle suction using strong attraction, as we have recounted such methods previously.
If it is not forgotten in this manner, then take a piece of alum or some prepared wax with which birds are caught, a small quantity of it, and place that at the tip of the nail, and then wrap around it decently placed. And then gently insert that into the ear canal with ease, and then dry the ear of moisture.
And if it is not forgotten among all the causes that we have recounted, then proceed to incise that one which becomes inflamed or spastic.
And the method of incising that one is to begin by cutting into the malaise of the sphenoidal vein and extract the blood according to the manner of its virtue, and then make a cut between your fingers and turn the ear upwards, and create a small fissure at the root of the ear towards the side most pressed upon. And let the fissure be of a lunar shape until it reaches the stone, and when the stone is found, remove it if you can with appropriate instruments; and then close the fissure at that moment quickly, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the cause lying in the ear is one of the growths that swell and become inflamed, then administer the treatment and the extraction of it; but if you do not adhere to this method, then take fine wax as follows: and administer the removal of that growth which has become lodged in the ear.
And do not proceed with this when you think that it has already become moistened by the vapor of the ear, and place it in small pieces, and then extract them either with hooks or with delicate forceps, or as we have said with incision, for the extraction will be easier.
Place in the water which the patient uses for sneezing at the beginning, and fill one ear with some old cloth, and lie down on that ear where there is the water, and if by this it comes out, good; but if not, now take smooth stones and lemons according to the length of a finger, and heat those in the fire a little, and the patient must put one of them into his ear canal and hop on that side and strike upon the stone with another stone. Then do not stop with the stone inside until all the water is out.
Then do not stop with the stone inside until all the water is out. And once the water is out in this way, take paper or cotton, and put one end of it into the ear and extend the other end toward the fire until most of it burns.
And put in another piece of cotton and do this as many times as necessary until all the water is out, and use a tube as mentioned above regarding the suction stone.
But in the extraction of an insect entering that ear, if it is a small thing, also painful and similar to what has been described, treat it as we have said in the division; but if it is a large object causing pain to the sensory nerve, now administer its removal by hand and with instruments, and the cause of this is more serious than all other causes that enter the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we have said in the division and in the treatise on distillations and your treatment does not provide comfort, now it is necessary that you expose the ear to the sun.
And thus if there is any cause for the worms, then at that time extract some medicine or very fine powder, but if no cause appears, then take a tube from below narrow and above wide, and this is its shape: And insert the fine end into the ear according to the amount that the patient can tolerate; and then suck out with strong suction, and do this several times until all the worms are expelled.
But if you do not wish to proceed with this method, then scrape and plug the area around the tube with wax as we have said; then administer distilled liquids that have already been tested for efficacy in treating worms, and you will find these described in the treatise on distillations.
And make your application with the instrument in which you pour the medicinal oils into the ear, and this is its shape: And make it of silver or iron, narrow at the bottom, in which there is a small hole, and with a flat top.
And thus it should be like a stopper at the end of the tube where it will be necessary. Take what is enclosed in the ear and heat it a little over fire. And be careful that the cause for which you will distill into the ear is not too harsh, for medicines for the ear do not tolerate this.
Chapter VII.
On the treatment of ear blockage.
In some cases, some children are born from their mothers' wombs with unopened ears, and this also happens in some adults, where the ear canal is blocked by pus or flesh growing there; and this blockage sometimes occurs at the entrance of the ear canal which cannot be seen clearly, and sometimes it is located in the outer parts.
And the one that occurs at the entrance of the ear canal, which cannot be seen clearly, is in the most difficult conditions to treat.
And in the one where you can see clearly, it is necessary to expose the ear of the patient to the sun and observe it. And if you see an apparent blockage, open it with a fine spatula of this form:
If the tip of it where the width is small, with one side sharp and the other not; and the spatula should be of two thin sides so as not to injure the ear.
