But if there is bleeding, then soak a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use another treatment that stops the flow of blood, which is described in the chapter on distillation.
Chapter VIII.
On the treatment of boils that occur on the eyelids.
Boils that occur on the eyelids sometimes are moist, and at other times they are dry.
Therefore, you should hold those with tweezers or scissors, and then cut them along their roots, and then place something powdered slightly on the spots.
If pain and bleeding come from a vein, then at that time you should cauterize those areas with a fine cautery near the anterior wall.
And the best cauterization is near incisions and wounds that return if any cause of their occurrence remains; and when they are cauterized with fire, those causes are burned up and do not return.
Chapter IX.
Treatment for a condition that affects the eyelids.
Wounds that remain in the eyelids due to some cause that is similar to a severe condition in its strength and hardness, and therefore are called severe.
And it is an accumulation of thick moisture in the eyelid, both above and below.
Therefore, the procedure is that you should now consider whether the condition appears on the surface of the eyelid and is aggravated in all parts; and the cause for this is light.
Therefore, place something over that area along the length of the fissure, and then carefully examine it from all parts with ease until you can see clearly, and then suspend it with an incision and bandage it.
And if the figure is large, then you should adjust it with stitches; and take care of it until it is healed.
And if it is small, you will not suffer harm from it because the warmth of the eye restores and heals it.
And if the swelling is declining toward the inner parts of the eyelid, you should lift the surface inside, then hold the eyelid and press upon it from all sides; and if the eyelid due to incision is elongated, this is not noted as a cause.
And after the incision, wash the wound with salt water; and treat the area with something that heals so that it may be healed.
Chapter 10.
On the treatment around the eye above the wound.
Axcirnath is gray and originates in the layers of the eyelid above, and it occurs more frequently in children, aggravating their eyes and causing them to suffer from excessive tearing because sunlight causes tears to form for them.
And for this reason they often rest their faces and sleep with them covered; and the eyelids of their eyes are wet under their eyebrows, which have been covered by inflammation, and a grayish appearance is apparent in the eye; and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel tenderness within.
And one method of treatment is for the patient to rest their head in your lap, and then place a cloth on them and make a circle according to the amount it contains of axcirnath from all sides, and then place that over it and press with your fingers all around so that grayish matter aggregates at the center of the circle, and then remove the humidity at the center with a spatula through a small incision, which should not be larger than the incision made in flaubotomy.
In the middle of the wound cut through the tendon completely until you reach the sinew, for in major dissections proceed to sever the sinew by a cleft according to the form of white flesh.
Then extract that from the cloths in which you have wrapped it between your said parts outside, and roll it between your right and left hand until it is dispersed.
And be careful not to press on the cleft so that the powder does not reach the wound and stain it.
But if the tendon does not appear at the beginning of the cleft, then press on the cleft slightly with ease until the tendon appears; and then extract it as we have said; and then immerse cloths in warm vinegar and place them over the site and sprinkle them with powders.
And some people sprinkle salt and place it in the concavity of the cleft so that what remains is dried by this humidity.
And then take care of it until it is healed.
But if an abscess occurs in the area, then treat it with soothing plasters until it is healed.

Chapter XII.
On the elevation of stinging hairs in the eye with an awl when the hair is hard.
When there are unnatural hairs, hard and rough and stinging the eyes, then the operation is that you take a fine needle and place it in that delicate soft hair, and then adjust the ends of it and grasp each one in a very subtle manner. And let the length of the strips be equal to the width of a palm.
And thereupon compose another fine strip below it in brief, and use its extremities if you so wish.
And then place the malady in its proper place according to the time, and do it near the sun so that your work may be evident, for it is a subtle and delicate operation.
And then insert the needle into the root of the hair that has been adjusted and which requires extraction, and make it penetrate quickly into the eyelid before you remove it from above the natural hair.
And then pull the hair up from above and insert it into an instrument, and let there be one or two or three hairs but no more.
And then pull your hand gently so that the hair is pulled out with the instrument onto the eyelid along with the natural hair.
But if it is pulled with the instrument and the hair is not extracted, pull the instrument to the sides below toward the strip that you have composed with it, so that it may be extracted from the tissue nearby.
And see that with the view of the eye, for now you will pull with the instrument and the strip, and then bandage the eye with cloths for two or three days until the hair adheres to its place and grows back on healthy tissue.
And if the hair is shorter than desired, now adjust a long hair from the natural hairs and place it there, or leave it for some time until it grows longer in length, and then lift it.
Chapter XIII.
On the treatment of a bruise that occurs on the upper eyelid.
When a bruise occurs on the eye and it is a contraction of the eyelid when it is closed, either to the right or left side, due to poor healing of wounds or cuts or cautery, and it is called leporine; this bruise can be natural or accidental, caused by poor healing of wounds or cuts or cautery, or similar malpractice.
The method of the procedure is that you should cut into that contraction and separate its edges, and place between them a piece of linen cloth, and bind it there until it heals.
And it is not advisable to use treatments for it that dry or constrict, as if you do this, the bruise will return worse than before and never heal properly.
Use treatments that soften, such as fenugreek, and boil the leaves and seeds in water, or use myrobalan, which should be dissolved in some oil, then soak a piece of cloth in it and apply it for treatment.
The goal of your treatment is to ensure that you do not allow any part to become excessively thickened or consolidated beyond its original form.
And this procedure that we have described is merely a correction of a bruise that does not return to its original form.
Chapter XIV.
On the treatment of a bruise that occurs on the lower eyelid.
This excrescence which is made in the lower eyelid, which is properly called an excrescence; and it is of two kinds, one natural, the other accidental.
The accidental one is caused by plague or by cutting or by cauterizing or by other similar means.
And the way of the operation is that you take a needle with which to make double thread, and place it in the flesh, and make it pass from the left tear duct into the right tear duct, until the thread reaches the two ends of the flesh.
And then stretch the flesh at the parts above the needle and cut along it with a broad spatula. And then if you return the shape of the eyelid as needed, it is good; and if not, take a rod and place it in the incised figure, and use that to convert the eyelid, and close the two openings at the bottom of the eyelid.
And let the ends of the two openings be at two corners of the incision that you have made where they meet, and let them form an angle such that when they come together, a figure is formed from them.
And this figure is the Greek letter: lambda.
And then remove the flesh there in the amount of the width below it, which dries the eyelid.
And then adjust the separated parts and stitch them together with linen thread; and afterwards treat those areas with what we have said about softening medicines and plasters until it is healed.
And if you decide on a cut, either through a fissure or suture or cautery, then now you must make a shallow incision above the eyelashes as is appropriate. And then separate the membrane between the two lips as we have said.
In the summary of the speech on the treatment of incisions when they are above or below, let it proceed according to the operation, as it is prepared in the form of an incision, and that varies in many ways.
And the skilled artisans always prepare in every possible way that is feasible within their ability to return the figure to its natural form or close to it, and they adapt according to the requirements of the operation and the instruments in the best arrangements.
Chapter XV.
On the adherence of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
When this consolidation occurs in some people such that the eyelid is firmly attached above the sclera and restricted in its movement and natural functions, then you must insert a probe below the eyelid, lift it up with it, or extend it to the root.
And then you should cut the adherence with a fine probe, which should not be sharp enough to cause cuts, but rather blunt so that the patient is not disturbed by the operation, and move the probe gently so as not to scratch the eye with its sharpness.
And your incision should be such that it easily separates the adherence so that the eyelid can return to its natural form, and ensure that the entire adherence is healed.
Then put in the wound salt water with which you wash; or distill in that of rose dissolved in water.

Chapter XVI.
On the swelling of the eyelid and the protrusion of flesh around the tear ducts.
Know that an eyelid swelling occurs in two ways: either it is nervous, and resembles a hard but subtle cicatrix; or it is non-nervous, resembling frozen white moisture, which when touched with a probe either readily takes hold of the probe and then releases it, or it is adherent and the probe cannot be inserted into it.
And both kinds do not begin except from the major tear duct, gradually spreading to cover the eye and prohibiting light and the movement of the eyelid.

And so you should follow it with the skin inside out.

Chapter XVII.
On incising an abscess and on what is born from flesh that has been tightly compressed in the area.
In some cases on the eyes of certain individuals, soft flesh grows and is pushed over until it covers the pupil, or approaches closely to the eyelids and strongly presses the lids together in parts inwardly and is gathered like a bollard.
The operation there is that the patient places his head in your lap; then open the eyes of that person and collect all that flesh at appropriate times, and with it use a forceps or a probe; then incise the flesh at the beginning until it is entirely done with the scalpel, and be careful not to damage the eye during your operation.

Chapter XIX.
On the reduction of nasal fractures.

Chapter XX.
On the Reduction of the Prominence of the Nose.
When the nose protrudes entirely and no harm or reduction occurs in vision, then you should allow the patient to rest, and subsequently apply windy poultices to the head without incision and use proper suction; and afterwards apply a liniment made of myrtle, aloe, juice, and sarcocolla to the nose, and then strengthen the plaster on the protruding nose with fine powder.
And the method for the powder is that you take linen cloth strips, and each strip should be folded into four layers; then make the powder from these strips.
And then apply those one upon the other according to the amount the nose requires. And then strengthen over those with a hard cloth tightly wrapped; and repeatedly apply the poultices with suction without incision; and then remove the poultices and leave the nose bound day and night.
But if meanwhile the bandage becomes loose, then it should be made tight again.
And then remove the bandage as we have said, both day and night.
And if you see the nose already reduced, that is good, and if not, then repeat the plaster with the powder and tight binding and the poultices until it is healed.

Chapter XXIV.
On the treatment of flesh wounds on the nose.
When on the nose there arise various flesh growths, some of which are caused by a condition similar to a scorpion made of moist flesh, or to a sea polyp; and this disease is called polypus because it resembles that sea creature. And it is cancerous flesh, ulcerated, of dark color; and there is also flesh that resembles a lion's but is of a dark color.
Therefore, for the one who has these flesh growths that are not fraudulent or cancerous, it is advisable to make the patient sit between your hands and place them in the sun, and starve the nose of it, and apply cold to those areas of the flesh. And if it is necessary to incise near the nose, do so with water or wine. But if the nose is swollen and the process of moisture spreads to the palate, then you should know that it has healed.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you will know that within there is flesh growing on the superfluous bones of the nose to which incision has not reached.
Then it is appropriate to introduce a probe into the area where there is any swelling, and cover it with linseed, placing an amount equal to the width of a finger or less between each application. And then pause the two ends of the probe, one end extending out over the nose and the other remaining inside the nose. Then administer a piece of flesh around the linseed that is on the probe, and do this until you know that the flesh has grown around the linseed on the probe.

Chapter XXV.
Of thorns that occur in the passages of the nose.
Most wounds occur in the extremities of the nose, and they are treated and dressed within a certain period during which one must take care of it.
And therefore it is necessary that you treat it at the beginning of its appearance and continue to take care of it entirely. And then place on the area the cautery or burning medicine that is to be used there.
But if the treatment is delayed until it needs to be treated, then consider whether it has become hard, dry, dark in color, and insensitive. At this point, do not touch it with a hot iron, for many times those who treat such an abscess incorrectly cause great harm to the patient.
And if the abscess is slightly touched and not dark in color, and treatment is possible at all times, then evacuate the pus and treat it without fear, and care for the area with substances that dry and constrict until it is healed.
Chapter XXVI.
On suturing the nose and lips and ear when continuity is lost due to plague or similar causes.
Know that when there is a loss of continuity in one of these cartilages, few people are capable of performing such an operation.
Therefore, when dealing with any cause of these issues, you must consider whether it is a fresh wound or due to the condition of the blood. Then join the two edges of the wound with stitches and subsequently care for it until it heals.
And if the lips of a wound of continuity are already separated, and each is made whole, now you must excise each part of its apparent edge until it comes out sound. And then adjust the two lips with sutures and strengthen them, and sprinkle over them powder of dragon’s blood and three-pennyworth of it, and place on the powder a piece of mastic plaster or some other consolidating plaster, and let that lie for two days or three, and then remove that and change the plaster and let it be until the pus is completely absorbed, and then cure with ointment until it is healed.
And the method of suturing is that you join the wound of continuity either with needles as we have recommended for the suturing of the abdomen, or join the wound of continuity with thread as it suits you and as you know how to do.
Chapter XXVII.
On the extraction of those causes that appear in the two lips in their parts within.
Often in some men in the inner parts of their lips there appear hard swellings similar to mutton fat, and some of them are smaller and some are larger.
Therefore you should turn out the lips and cut into each of those hardnesses from all sides, and then when you remove it, fill the place with fine linen until the blood is congealed, and then wash it with vinegar water and treat the area with something astringent until it is healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
Of the incision of flesh adjusted to the gums.
Great care must be taken when dealing with flesh attached to the gum. The ancients called this condition albulus. Then you should suspend the affected area for about an ounce or hold it in forceps and incise it to its base, allowing pus or blood to flow out.
And then apply pressure to the affected area with a styptic or drying powder.
And if the flesh regrows, now incise it again and cauterize it, for after cauterization, it will not return.
Chapter XXIX.
Of the extraction of teeth with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth, both inside and outside, and between the attached gums, there are rough, sharp edges, lumps, and in some cases dark spots that make the teeth appear yellow or green, leading to corruption of the gum due to this condition.
And therefore, you must have the patient sit between your hands and hold his head in such a way that you can rasp the teeth on which these rough edges appear until no roughness remains, thus removing the darkness and yellowness and other conditions so they may be cleansed.
If this can be done at the beginning of the extraction, that is well; but if not, then you should repeat the extraction on those teeth on another day and on the second and third days until it reaches its final stage as you wish.
And know that teeth have need of various instruments of different shapes and figures according to what is prepared for your operation. And therefore take the razors with which the tooth is cut from its inner side as another razor with which it is rasped between the teeth according to other forms, and these forms of razors you should have prepared, which you will see shortly:
Chapter xxx.
On the extraction of teeth.
When you wish to cure the tooth of its pain by totally removing it and delaying in total extraction, for there is no cause that will compensate for its absence when it is extracted, for it is a substance sublime wherein there is excuse in the extraction of it.
But when the patient is forced to have that tooth extracted, you must be firmly certain which is the painful tooth, for often the pain deceives the patient and seems to be in a healthy tooth, and if you extract this one, soon the pain does not cease until the diseased and painful tooth is removed, as we have already seen by the operation of barbers.
Therefore, you must be certain which is the painful tooth. Once you know well, then you should scarify around the tooth with spatula that has some virtue until it dissolves the tooth from the gum completely, and then use your fingers or fine forceps gradually little by little until it loosens.
And then grasp it firmly for good traction or strong extraction, and place your hand behind the patient's head so that you hold it well without hurting them, and then pull the tooth straight in such a way that you do not break it, nor cause greater pain to the patient.
And so if it is not disunited, take one of those instruments and place it below all around with ease and exert effort to remove it as it was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is loose or decayed, now you must cover that part with cloth and secure it tightly at the end with a fine thread so that it does not break during your compression on that area to hold it.
And you must work carefully around the incision in the entire circumference of the gum and be mindful of your strength so that you do not break any part of it, and return to the diseased area for the purpose of alleviating the pain more effectively than the initial discomfort.
And be careful not to do what some ignorant barbers do in their rashness and audacity during the removal of teeth, without administering what we have recommended; because many times they cause great harm to sick people which are harder to heal, and the roots of those teeth become rotten completely, or partially, or they extract it along with some bones of the mandible as many times we have witnessed.
And after the extraction of the tooth, the mouth should be washed with warm water or salt water.
And if there is bleeding due to this, as often happens, now take some quantity of sugar and sprinkle it over the wound, and if that does not suffice, cauterize it.
The shape of the forceps and delicate holders with which the tooth is grasped at the beginning should be of long extremity, short handle, and thick, so that they are not bent when you must work with them.
And so they should be like pieces of thick cloth that when you stretch them out are not folded, but their extremities should be short.
And they should be made of Indian iron, straight at the extremities, and at those extremities there should be teeth, some of which enter into others and stretch out by strong constriction, and sometimes the extremities of these should have the shape of a bird's beak called a ciconia, that is a hawk, and they are of very strong constriction.

Chapter xxxii.
On the treatment of prominent teeth over other teeth.
When the prominent teeth do not follow their natural course and especially when this affects women, and particularly those of venerable age; then you should consider: if a tooth is too close to another tooth and it is impossible to extract or apply medication, in that case, root it out. But ensure it does not damage the adjacent tooth; therefore, you incise it with this instrument which has the following form; it resembles a boar's tusk and is made of Indian steel with an extremely sharp point.
And ensure your incision is such that when you cut it, it does not take more than three days due to the hardness of the tooth, and also make sure it does not infect any other teeth.
And if it is a prominent tooth that requires filing, then file it with an Indian steel file which has this form:
And let it be entirely made of Indian steel and use it carefully; let it have very sharp points as with a file used for needles, with which the tooth is filed bit by bit over several days with ease, so that no other tooth becomes infected or damaged. And afterward clean it at the end, and polish it with some razors.
And if it is a tooth where part of it is broken and you run your tongue over it, then you should file down the rough edges until they are smooth and even, and fill it in so that your tongue does not get injured and the speech is not impaired.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the restoration of defective teeth with gold or silver paste.
When it happens that the front teeth are damaged by impact or displacement due to an accident or fall, and the patient cannot chew properly as a result, and you have already treated them with styptic medicines but your treatment has not been effective, now since they cannot be retained otherwise, implant those which are robust with gold paste and not silver, for silver changes color and soon becomes apparent, whereas gold never changes.
And let the paste be in such subtlety and thickness according to the quantity that can fit between the teeth; and the form of the restoration is that you take the paste and place twice as much between two healthy teeth, and then increase the amount of the paste between either one or multiple defective teeth until it reaches the adjacent healthy tooth on the other side. And then continue the application at the place where you started, and strengthen your work with ease and skill so that it is not defective.
And when you apply the paste, make sure to press it against the roots of the teeth so that it does not escape.
And then cut off the excess paste with forceps or scissors, and shape it carefully in each place, and fold it so that it is hidden between the healthy tooth and the defective one, so that it does not injure the tongue. And then make that part as smooth as a Roman coin.
But if it is loose or broken, strengthen that part with additional paste in such a way over time.
This is the form of the teeth and the figure of the arrangement of two healthy teeth and two defective teeth, as you see these lined up:
And when it happens that one or two teeth are returned to their proper place near the gum, and they grow back as we have recounted, and remain in place.
And only a good artisan and wise person can do this.
And sometimes a substitute is made from the bones of a cow for certain reasons, and it is fashioned in the shape of a tooth, and placed in the place where the tooth is missing, and it is secured as we have said, and remains and serves well for a long time.
Chapter XXXIV.
On the incision of the membrane that is in the tongue and prevents speech.
This membrane that is under the tongue sometimes is natural and one is born with it, or it is accidental and occurs due to an injury that has already healed.
The operation there is that one opens the mouth of the patient, with their head positioned appropriately; and raises their tongue, and then cuts that membrane along the nerve according to its width, ensuring the tongue's mobility.
But if there is any hardness or knot in that area, and it is due to the healing of an injury, then you should gently cut through it along its width until the knot is dissolved.
And be careful not to create a tear in the surface of the flesh so that you do not cut into the arteries and cause bleeding.
After washing the remnants of the incision with water or vinegar or with water and a cloth, place a clean linen pad underneath the tongue; this is the method by which the bad taste under the tongue should be placed there each night so that it does not become consolidated elsewhere.
If bleeding occurs, place over the area vitriol powder; but if the blood flows continuously, then cauterize the area with a hot iron, which is suitable for this condition. And after that, treat with the other causes until it is healed.
Chapter XXXV.
On the extraction of a sublingual cyst.
Sometimes, below the tongue, there appears a swelling similar to a small abscess that prevents the tongue from performing its natural function; and quickly it enlarges until it fills the mouth.
The operation to be performed is to open the mouth wide in front of the light and examine the swelling.
And if you see it as a dark or black color, hard, and one that does not feel pain when touched, then do not touch what is clearly gangrenous.
And if it is inclined toward a whiteness with moisture, then you should proceed by making an incision with a fine scalpel, and cut it completely; and if blood flows during your operation, then place over it powdered zinc sulfate, which is vitriol, to stop the bleeding.
And then return to your work once you have treated it thoroughly, washing it with vinegar and salt; and continue treating it with other appropriate methods until it is healed.
Chapter XXXVI.
On the extraction of an abscess from both armpits and one on the groin and other abscesses.
When you find in the armpit a swelling that consists of glands which are assembled together and appear outside, known as armpits, when you treat them as we have said in the division and they do not heal, then consider whether the abscess is hard, dark-colored, with little sensation; at this point, do not touch it with iron.
And if it is red in color and the root is thick, then do not touch it with iron due to the flow of blood; instead, leave it inside until it ripens, and then either you will extract it or it may burst on its own.
And if it is white in color, soft, and the root is delicate, then that one should be incised.
And the operation on it is that you consider, before your operation, whether the abscess has already rested sufficiently after being heated or if it has diminished in any way; then have the patient sit facing the sunlight, and place their head to your right, and expose the mouth of the abscess, and the assistant should hold between his fingers the tongue made of lead below this instrument, which has the following form:
And it should be made of silver or iron, and should be as thin as a knife.
When you press with that tongue and the abscess is shown to you, place your finger firmly over it, then take some of it and put it in an almond, and pull it outwards as much as you can. Other than that, if you want to cut any part of the tunics, and appropriately incise them with an instrument which has this form:
Other than that, if you want to cut any part of the tunics, and appropriately incise them with an instrument which has this form: and it is similar to forceps, but the extremities of it are curved, so that the opening of each of those is in line with the other, sharp, which is made of iron.
But if this instrument is not at hand, then incise it with a spatula which has this form: It is sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you have made an incision, make the other one according to this method of incising, and then wash the wound with fresh water.
But if there is bleeding, wash it with decoction of lemon peel or mint leaves and similar astringent substances until the flow of blood is stopped, and then proceed to treat it until it is healed.
And sometimes there appear in the throat other abscesses or those two armpits, and incise those as I have said regarding the incision of two wounds similarly.
And I had treated a woman with an abscess that was located in her throat, which abscess tended to have a dark color and little sensation, and it had already closed off her throat, so the woman could only breathe through a narrow passage. She had been prohibited from eating or drinking water, and she would have reached death if I had delayed for even one or two more days.
And the abscess had burst so much that there were two passages through which it drained out by two tracts.
Then I cut it surgically and placed it in one of their vessels, and pulled that out, and from there removed the large portion, and then I incised that part to understand the other side, and then I closed its mouth, and compressed its tongue, and then I placed the vessel in that abscess.
And then I removed a large part of it, and only a little blood came from it. Then the woman's condition improved completely, and she began to drink water right away, and soon after ate food. Therefore, I did not stop incising that abscess for many days, and another abscess succeeded in its place, which I incised until it was extended fully.
Then I applied something and cauterized the wound of it, because of excessive addition; and then I wandered around and did not know what God would do next.
Chapter XXXVII.
On the incision of the breast when it is abscessed.
When one notices catarr in the breast, and it is abscessed and elongated, it is then called perpendicular; and if it is thick and round in the lower part, it is then called a bubba.
However, when it is cured as we have described in the division, and you do not see your recovery, and similarly if an abscess has been cauterized but remains solid and subtle, then it is necessary for you to incise those that are aggregated and round; and if they are long and sanguineous, of dark or black color, and not at the center, then it is necessary for you to attempt its incision, because there is fear of malady in them.
And when you see it according to the method that I have said about its whiteness and length, then you should make the patient sit down and press the tongue with an instrument of which the form is appropriate, and place the incision in the new area, and pull it downwards and cut it with one of two instruments which are mentioned in the cutting of two layers.
And you should not cut it more than what is adjusted to the natural limit without addition, for if you cut too much, you will harm their speech.
And after the incision, use what we have recommended for the incision of two layers, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by the incision, then use something to cauterize it without causing fear, and the method of cauterization is nothing but with sharp water. Place the head of the patient on your lap, and then press the tongue with the instrument that we have mentioned, and then take the water which we have described in the chapter on cauterization, and apply it to the area so that it becomes heated, and place it neither too thick nor too thin, and with this fill the concavity of the instrument whose form is as follows:
Let the extremity of the cauterized area be like the concavity of a spoon, in which the medicine is placed, and place the instrument with the medicine on the palate, and the patient should lie down so that saliva or mucus from the mouth does not flow out due to the internal medicine, so that none of it escapes and you can retain it.
And then with your hand on the medicine, apply it to the uvula for a short period until you see it become blackened and numb due to the bite of the medicine.
And if you want, take a plug and wrap it around the extremity of the vein, and immerse the plug in the medicine, and insert the vein with the plug into a tube so that the plug adheres to that tube, and do this as many times as necessary until you achieve what you want from the cauterization of the grape, and then leave it so that it dries and falls off after three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, do so, and then for the cauterization, clean around the grape with a plug embedded at the border, and modify what is in the circuit of it with the medicine, and wash it with rinsing water, and treat from outside with embrocation, and proceed with cautery inside until it is healed.
And this method of treatment is safer than incision and more enduring without fear.
In some cases, the grape is cured with something that cauterizes and incises more subtly. It is treated with sublimation in this manner: take cerate, hyssop, thyme, rue, Arabian saffron, chamomile, and similar herbs, and adjust all these ingredients or some of them in a pot, which are immersed in sour wine and boiled; the pot should be heated moderately, and at the bottom of the pot place a tube through which an instrument made of wire is suspended in this manner; it can be made of silver or wire.
And insert the extremity into the mouth of the malate so that the vapor reaches the uvula through the tube, until the uvula is sufficiently treated.
And then proceed with those incisions into where it is dry, and be careful not to perform this treatment at the beginning of the onset of abscesses, for it would be too early for the abscess, and you should not do that, but rather wait for the formation and development of the abscess.
But if this instrument is not available to you, now take a cane and attach one end of it with a piece of cloth, so that the mouth of the wound is covered, for the cloth prevents the vapor from reaching and burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter XXXVIII.
On the extraction of thorns and of what adheres to the gums and other causes.
Incisions are made in what adheres to the gum or a fish bone or some other cause.
Then you should extract what is visible among those things, upon which your eye rests, and then press the tongue with an instrument in front of light, so that it becomes clear to you what is in the gum.
And what is not visible and hidden in the gum, you should make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, for the cause attached to the gum will easily be expelled by vomiting; or have the patient swallow a piece of bread or lettuce leaves; or take a piece of dry sea sponge, soak it strongly in vinegar, and then let the patient swallow that, as when it reaches the place where the thorn is, it will easily be expelled. Take an eel and boil it in vinegar, then remove the inside and extract the jelly; or take a poultice and apply the jelly to it and with the jelly treat it.
If you do not forget the causes that we have mentioned, now use an instrument of lead according to this form:
And let it be as thick as a small reed, and at the end of it let there be a curve which you insert into its throat with ease.
And as he raises his head up high, and be careful not to touch the bones of the throat, so that it does not harm him or those who pull the bone or spine, or that the physician touches it.
However, let the patient who inserts it be skilled, knowing the location of the cause and pulling it down, and pull his hand with the instrument up, all this according to the quantity that is prepared within until it is forgotten.
Chapter XXXIX.
On the extraction of a bloodsucker attached to the throat.
When treating a bloodsucker as we have said in the division of treatment with medicines and not compresses, consider now the throat of the patient in the sunlight, since you have protruded the tongue of the patient with the instrument that we have recommended.
Then if you see the bloodsucker, remove it carefully with a small incision or with subtle gestures appropriately.
And if it is not possible for you to remove it then, now take a concave cannula and insert it into the patient's throat, and immediately place in the concavity of the cannula an ignited iron, and do this many times (until the bloodsucker dies and falls off) and keep the patient from water for the entire day.
Take a clean jar full of water and rinse the mouth with it, and wash the mouth with it; and do not spill any of it for any reason and keep the water in the mouth for an hour one after another as soon as the bloodsucker feels the water cold.
But if it is not possible to do as we have recommended with this, then fumigate the malodor with cimex or with asafetida by means of the instrument which we have recommended for the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, so that it may fall out.
And there is another method of operation in fumigation involving this: take an olive in which are burning coals and the olive is covered with a cover in the middle of which is a hole, and attach the end of the instrument to that hole; then proceed with the fumigation from above; and have the patient place his mouth on the end of the tube, and close his mouth so that the fumigation does not escape until you know that the fumigation has reached the bloodsucker, for the bloodsucker will fall out at once.
And if it does not fall out, then repeat the fumigation several times, and abstain the patient from drinking, and give salty foods and herbs, and do not let him drink water; and in this way it is necessary for it to be expelled.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the bloodsucker is treated when it is seen in the throat; and it resembles forceps as you see them, but with some curvature: which is placed in the throat, and the end of it resembles the beak of a stork, which is sharpened with a file, so that when it grasps something, it does not let go.
Chapter XL.
In this chapter is the summary of the speech on the preparation for abscesses and their incision.
Abscesses are of various types and manners, as will be recalled for each one in this book.
And they differ in their puncture and incision by two methods, of which one is according to the type of abscess itself; and by what they draw from the humors.
And the second method is according to the places in which they occur in the body, for an abscess that occurs in the head is different from one that occurs in the buttocks; and an abscess that happens in a fleshy part is different from one that happens at a joint; and each has its treatment by operation.
And there are some abscesses that should not be punctured but await proper maturation of the pus before being treated.
There are other abscesses that should be evacuated when they are crosswise and have not matured according to their proper time, such as those near joints, because an abscess near a joint can extend until you puncture what is around it, and the ligaments or nerves of that joint may be corrupted if you delay, causing contraction of that limb; or it is an abscess near a principal member, and if you delay in puncturing it before it matures, it will cause harm to the principal member; or it is near the buttocks, so puncture it while it is not fully matured; but do not puncture it raw when digestion is incomplete, except for those near the buttocks, because if you wait too long, pus may be elongated into deeper parts of the buttocks and a fistula may form, or it may reach a state where healing cannot occur. May you know the time for piercing the abscess, and this is when it is ripe.
And you should know that it is ripe when there is relief from pain and the cessation of fever, and shortly after a decrease in inflammation and pulse, and the sharpness at the head of the abscess, and other signs.
And you should know that the piercing should be made in the place where the pus is deepest within the abscess and coming from the elevation.
And the piercing should always be made along the length of the flesh or body if the abscesses are in the areas of the chest or back, and in places like joints, tendons, nerves, arteries; and finally, in all other equal places where there is no reflection; for those that do reflect, use a piercing according to that member.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy area, the best time is to delay the piercing until after the digestion of the second stage has been confirmed, as we have said; for if you pierce it before this, the course of the illness will be prolonged and more severe, and the lips of the wound will become hardened and its depth greater.
But some abscesses should be pierced across the grain of the flesh or body due to necessity, or according to what is required by the member.
And you should administer a small piercing, and in large abscesses, a larger and wider piercing or multiple sections according to the diversity of the size of the abscesses.
And other abscesses in which the treatment should be hollowed out and incised, when it is done as said before, are performed at a time when they have already become established, just as those that occur from excessive dubius (a type of swelling) and buboes.
And of those which are incised with a three-angled section; and of those in which it is incised as the figure of a myrtle leaf, just like the abscesses of the angles.
And of those in which a round section and a lunar section are administered, and similar to these.
But in the case where there is none of the above, as with superficial lion abscesses, you should puncture it with a superficial incision or just plane it.
And when the abscess is large and has collected pus, you should puncture it but do not hesitate to extract all the pus at that moment; having extracted part of it, then scrape the abscess on another day, and then extract a larger portion, and continue doing this over several days according to its degree of maturity.
And especially if the patient is a sick man, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an elderly person in frailty, because their vital spirit quickly dissipates with the discharge of the pus, and the patient easily dies, so you should treat them carefully.
Therefore, guard this chapter with great caution, and when you puncture these abscesses, make sure to clean the plaques and then consider whether the plaque of the abscess is small or if it is a flat section; in that case, administer some of the following remedies: either from wool or ant honey. And if the abscess is large and the fissures of the puncture are many, then place a piece of cloth in each one until they coalesce.
And if part of the cord is severed in the abscess or you have concaved it around the circumference, then you should fill it with old cloth or thread made from fabric, as when you treat ulcers or wounds without moisture; and strengthen that on the third day; then proceed to care for it using what is appropriate among the ointments until it is healed.
If blood flows during the time of your operation, then prepare water and vinegar in which to dip a cloth, and place that over the site of the bleeding several times.
If the bleeding persists, then proceed to administer regimen and treatment with powders which I have recommended in various places of this our book and division.
If it is in the time of winter, and the site of the abscess is a place with many nerves, then proceed to mix powders in vinegar and burnt oil, and apply those to the sites.
And if it is in summer, and the sites are fleshy, then proceed to apply powders soaked in water and oil or in vinegar and oil, and let all of this be applied for three days as we have said, and then you should open the abscess, cleanse it, and administer whatever is needed for its treatment until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses generally, but according to the method of distinction I have already said about every abscess in what manner is the way of its cure separately by chapters.
Chapter XLIV.
On the sectioning of abscesses that occur in the neck.
In the neck, small abscesses may occur, and there are various types of nodes that contain membranes within them; these types vary in many ways.
And they are soft, and the contents of these are moist, similar to lime. Of those among which I contain humidity similar to dust, and resembling them; and of those that are stony, hard.
And in the section of all, and in the extraction of those there is no fear, as the extraction of those does not oppose the arteries.
And the operation in the section of those is that you press them with an instrument whose form is similar to this, which is called an entromes, until you know what it contains.
For if what it contains is humidity, then cut that with a section according to its length by making a superficial section according to this form:
And begin the section from the line of .B. to the line of .G. Then, as the humidity is evacuated, you should excise that skin which contains the humidity, and do not leave any part of it behind, for if any part remains, inflammation may return.
And then immerse the wound in Egyptian ointment if it is available, and if not, in salt water; and fill the wound with this mixture and leave it for another day, for in this way you will dissolve all that remains of the membrane.
And then apply the wound again with Egyptian ointment a second and third time if necessary, until you are confident that there is no remaining part of the membrane; now treat the wound with the ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains hard lumps, then you should incise it according to this form: with a section having the shape of a cross, and insert needles between two lips of the section and scrape it thoroughly from all sides, and exert your skill in extracting the membrane that contains it.
If there is opposition to what I have said about surgery, now pay attention to what I have recounted first.
And similarly, deal with the mature abscess by incision and treatment as we have told you; and the incision of a mature abscess is easier, because it contains little blood and less moisture.
And sometimes there are abscesses in the head of certain people who do not have much moisture; the cause is that I incised an abscess on an old woman's head and found it as hard as a stone, rough, white, which could not be softened, and if anyone were to encounter such a situation and struck it, they would harm themselves.

Chapter XLII.

Chapter XLIX.
On the incision of abscesses that occur due to arterial or venous causes.
When an artery is ruptured and the surrounding area has consolidated, abscesses may occur. The signs by which it can be known whether inflammation and abscess are due to an artery or a vein: for when an abscess is due to an artery, it tends to aggregate in the front part of the body; and when you press on the abscess with your fingers, you feel a rustling sound there.
And that which is from a vein is round at the surface of the body.
And a section on this abscess is an error, especially in those areas beyond the armpits, and in the angles, and in the neck, and in many places on the body; for it is dangerous, therefore it must be avoided that its treatment with fire be shunned, and those that are in the extremities or on the head, must also be avoided.
But for that which is due to inflammation of the artery opening, divide it in a longitudinal section; and then carefully make a section with hooks near the artery, and free the tunics until they are exposed.
And then place on that needle and make it penetrate into the other side, and tie the artery with a double thread in two places, which method I have learned from the extraction of two arteries, which were in the thighs.
And then press the place between two bandages with a sponge so that the blood comes out completely while it is there, and the abscess dissolves.
And then administer treatment in which poultices are applied so that the bandages can be removed; and then treat it with appropriate ointments until it is healed.
And if it is an abscess due to a vein section, now you should have in your hand what is possible of the abscess with the longitudinal approach, and then insert the needle below the place where you had your hand, and make it penetrate; and tie it there doubly so that you can extract it from the other side, and then ligate the abscess with a strong bandage as I have told you in the grapevine ligature on both sides of the filament's ends.
If you fear that you will extract the thread, then at that moment insert a needle with some of that cautery below the abscess; you should first make an incision with the tip of the needle, and strengthen its points in four places.
Apply dry dressings to the abscess in the middle part of the area until what is inside has come out; then you should incise the excess skin, and leave what is attached.
And then apply powder that you have soaked in wine and oil, and proceed with the treatment using cauterization and herbs until it is healed.
Chapter I of the abscess that occurs due to the twisting of nerves.
Just as an abscess can occur in arteries and veins, similarly an abscess occurs in the nerves when there is a percussion injury or from excessive strain and by analogy.
And it is done with care in the areas of the heel or anywhere where the joints are often strained.
And it is a severe abscess, and its color resembles that of the surrounding tissue, and it is among the worst conditions when it occurs with vehemence, and the patient appears dazed.
And it is not an abscess that aggregates at the front of the body, and therefore it is below the skin, and it spreads to all parts without going forward or backward.
Therefore, when it is in the joints, it should not be wrapped in metal, for it will quickly lead to contraction.
Take him who has it in the head or in the forehead with a bandage and spatum. If it is a small abscess, open it with forceps and excise it along the edges.
And if it is large, then suspend it with incisions, and scrape it out; and afterward close the wound with stitches, and treat it until it is healed.

Chapter LIＩ.
On the Treatment of an Umbilical Hernia.
An umbilical hernia is caused by various reasons; it can be due to fissures in the abdominal wall above the belly, and for this, a patch made of cloth or gut is applied as in other ruptures; or it can be from a wound through which blood flows from a vein or artery depending on what is present there, or it can be caused by trapped air.
If it is through a crack in the hip joint and due to tearing of the tissue, then the color of the abscess resembles the color of the skin; it causes severe pain, and soon appears in various situations. And if it is due to the protrusion of the bowel, then its situation differs greatly from what we have described about other cases; when you press on that area with your fingers, it becomes hidden and soon returns. It is very light in weight and is greatly magnified after bathing and hard work.
If it is due to moisture, then it remains visible and does not hide when you press on it with your hand; it is not constricted nor hardened.
And if it is due to blood, then these signs appear and the abscess turns to a dark blackish color.
If it is due to excess flesh, then the abscess is severe, large and hard, and its size varies according to the amount.
If it is due to gas, then the touch of it remains soft.
And the operation in this case is that you must consider whether the prominence of the navel is due to blood from an artery, or a vein, or gas; now it is advisable for you to be cautious about treating it, because there is fear and concern as I have taught you in the chapter where I mentioned abscesses from arteries and veins.
And if the prominence of the navel is due to the bowel or tissue, then it is advisable for you to instruct the patient to lie on their back and extend themselves; then feel around the entire circumference of the navel. Then instruct them to stand with their back between your hands on both sides; subsequently encircle the area with a bandage around the circumference of the navel where you felt it; then extend half of the abscess upwards by about an inch; then wrap it in a strong thread or soft cloth and tie it securely.
From the side of the abscess, lift the membrane that extends over the bandage and insert your finger into the pus and search for the bag; and if you have found it or secured it with the bandage, then immediately extract it and push the bag to the parts inside the abdomen.
And if you have found the membrane, then grasp it with forceps; and incise the excess tissue around it.
But if it is contrary to your artery or vein, then surround that spot, and return to your operation; take two needles and place them in those two strong points and insert two needles into the circuit you made around the abscess in a cross pattern, which should penetrate; and then cut the abscess into four parts over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the area open so that the extended flesh can drain and fall off by itself; or incise it when it is softened and will drain, and then treat it with a liquid from the herbs until it heals.
And if the prominence of the navel is of flesh or due to humidity, then you should proceed to treat the abscess as we have said and remove the blood or moisture that you find there, and then treat it with what is appropriate to heal and consolidate the area.

And in some cases, it is treated with iron according to this method: the patient lies on their back between two chairs, and then the area of the rupture of the swelling is dried by cutting across its width as needed.
And then stretch the white skin that is below the groin until it is exposed.
Take the knife and place it above the prominent part of the navel, and press it against the belly.
And then cut the two prominent places at the end of the line of the navel, and join one to the other with a suture.
And then extract the end of the knife, and do not incise the navel completely, nor extend the opening, nor for any other reason as I will explain to you in the treatment of an intestinal hernia.
And then treat it as you would a wound, and when you incise the flaps, clean them and restore the wound until it is healed; for the navel is contracted and the prominence of rupture has not been adjusted.
And cauterization is better in this place, for it is closer to healing.
Chapter LXVIII.
On the softening of the cord of the testicles.
Often the cord of the testicles in some men is softened because the tissues within it are softened, and this requires careful attention.
Therefore, it is necessary for the one who is asked to treat this condition to have the patient lie on his side, and incise the entire cord that is softened over the natural cord; and then join the two edges with a suture.
And if you wish, cut the excess of the softened cord at three or four places and suture it securely.
And then incise between the sutures; and afterward treat it as you would other wounds until it is healed and the flaps fall.
Chapter Lxix.
On Castration.
Know that castration in our law is excommunicated, but for a reason it is read to me that I should remember it in this book; and the remembrance of it is not necessary except for two ways, of which one is so that when a physician is asked about it, he knows how to treat it when it occurs; and the other way is that we have sometimes needed castration in other animals for our use by them, such as horses, oxen, and pigs, and similar beasts.
And thus I say that castration is performed in two ways: either by attrition or by incision.
But in the one that is performed by attrition, the method of the operation is that we should bathe the animal in warm water until the testicles become softened and descend. And then take those [testicles] in your hand until they become soft and do not appear to be hurt by the touch.
But in castration performed by incision and cutting, make sure that you hold the animal and press the scrotum of the testicles with your left hand.
And then lift the suspensory ligaments, and make an incision on each side with a cut so that two sides are processed.
And then incise those parts that you have each exposed. And do not leave any cause for infection on those parts, but make the incision subtle so that it is above the veins.
And this method of castration is better, which is done by attrition, for attrition leaves some cause of the testicles whereby the beast desires sex.
And properly treat the plague until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
On the treatment of hermaphrodism.
Hermaphroditism in men occurs in two ways: one is when there appears in the space between the testicles, where there should be hair like that of a woman.
And sometimes there is a passage through which urine flows, located in the space between the testicles.
In women, there is one type: the organ of the woman above the pubis is like two testicles of a man, slightly towards the front as with a man's scrotum and two testicles.
And the treatment is of three types: two for men and one type for women. And it is that you should incise those tight tissues until the pus escapes, and then treat them as you would other wounds.
And in the second type for men, where urine flows from the space between the testicles, there is no treatment or operation needed.
Chapter 21.
On the treatment of protrusions and elevated flesh due to causes related to women.
Let the woman be laid on her back in a natural position, and let it be adjusted so that it is fit for examination; and in some women certain parts are enlarged to such an extent that they spread as much as in men and prevent intercourse.
Then take the superfluous part of the woman in your hand or with forceps and incise it.
And do not limit the incision, especially at the root of the hair, so that it does not cause a flow of blood.
And then treat her with the care needed for wounds until she is healed.
Hidden flesh is the flesh that grows in the crevice of the mother where it fills that area, and it often lies in the parts outside according to the appearance of a tail (and thus some call this "tailed illness"); therefore, we must incise it as we would incise the woman's flesh, and treat her until she is healed.
Chapter LXXII.
On the Treatment of Rabies.
Rabies is characterized by the woman's vagina not being perforated or the passage being narrow. And it can be either natural or accidental.
It is accidental due to a disease that preceded it, and that condition is caused either by flesh that is not properly adjusted or by thin or thick secretions.
And it occurs either at the root of the uterus, or on its sides, or in its upper part, or in its lower part, and you must prohibit intercourse and conception and childbirth, and often you must also prevent menstruation.
Of that knowledge is made by observation of the pupil, if the opacity is apparent, proceed; but if it is not apparent, then you should around that with your fingers or with light.
But if the opacity is due to a fine membrane and it extends to both sides, then you should cut and disrupt that.
And when you place small probes on both sides, then place your fingers indicating the most intimate parts.
And let the woman lie on her back, with her legs spread apart, and then spread the two lips forcefully so that the fine membrane is ruptured and the opacity is exposed.
And then take some lint and soak it in oil and place that on the area, and instruct the patient to leave it covered for a day so that the cut does not become sealed.
But if the membrane is thick and dense, then you should proceed to cut it with a scalpel or myrtle leaf.
But if the opacity is due to tissue growth, then suspend that for a moment, and incise it.
And have with you the medicines that stop blood flow without causing harm, such as hystriomnion, dragon's blood, and cyprus, mixed with egg white.
And then administer a lead tube that is wide so that the wound does not heal too quickly.
And keep that for several days and administer dry powder.
And it should be treated along with the other treatment for consolidation until it is healed.
And in some cases it happens at the major birth of other tissues, and then it is necessary to incise according to this method. But if this is due to a chronic abscess, the chronic abscess which is in the major part should not be treated contrary in any way with iron.
Chapter LXXIII.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids and tumors that occur in the genital area of women.
Hemorrhoids are appreciated as the enlargements of the openings of small veins from which blood flows continuously; and when these old hemorrhoids are formed, they become hard.
And now I have mentioned in the division the kinds of those and the signs of those, and to say which ones receive treatment and which ones do not receive treatment.
Therefore, I say that hemorrhoids and nodes when they are in the vicinity of the major part and do not appear to the touch, then in those no treatment should be done with iron.
But those that are at the opening of the major part and which can be felt by touch, these are the ones that are treated.
And it is necessary to place the woman in a suitable position and then stretch out those parts with forceps or with coarse cloth, and incise those with scissors.
And then powder should be applied to those places to promote the flow of blood from one of the incising powders by mastication, such as acacia and similar ones.
And then hold the pieces in cold water for a short time.
And if the bleeding persists, then apply it to a decoction of sumach, lemon rinds, gallnuts, and similar substances.
And then take flour of barley and mix it with honey and vinegar to make a plaster, which you should apply to the back.
And if the blood is clotted, that is good; but if not, apply two cupping glasses, and perform incisions at the mammary glands.
And then take wool and soak it in the juice of cilantro or anise or germander in sour wine, and apply it to the affected area; and afterwards treat the area with ointments until it is healed.
And treat it until it is healed.

Chapter LXXV.
To instruct midwives in how to treat unborn babies when there is an abnormal condition, as shown by the figure.

Chapter LXXVI.
On the Extraction of a Dead Fetus.
When you are treating a dead fetus as we have mentioned in the division of the medication and it has not yet been expelled, and you must necessarily use an operation at this time, then it is necessary to consider whether the woman is of sound constitution and not subject to any of those accidents by which danger to her is feared.
At this time, the woman should be placed on her back on the bed with her hips raised and her head lowered.
The minister should hold her by both legs or tie her to the bed so that her body does not extend during the extraction of the fetus.
And then you should moisten the mother's mouth with oils, honey, mucus of oatmeal and fenugreek, and linseed.
And then the midwife should anoint her hand with those oils and mucus, and then gently insert it into the mother, and then search with the hand for the location where the hooks can be placed in the fetus.
And here are appropriate locations: then you should consider whether the fetus is positioned so that it will descend with its head first, and at this time you place the hooks in the hair of it, or in the neck, or in the mouth, or in the palate, or under the chin, or in the clavicle, or in proper places on the sides of the ribs, or in the hypochondria.
But if the descent is with its feet first, then in the buttocks, or in the middle of the back.
Take the needle with the right hand and place its curve in that part which is between the fingers of the left hand, and insert it with ease into one of those places we have mentioned, until it reaches the diseased area.
And then place and insert another needle beside it, or a third if necessary, so that there is attraction equal on both sides, and not extension according to its straightness alone.
And do this to all parts, so that the extraction of it is eased just as it is done with teeth during their extraction.
And apply to the part where the extension needs to be softened; and if any part is stuck, then it is necessary for the midwife to anoint some of those parts with oil and insert them carefully and rotate what is stuck.
Then if the part of the fetus is dislodged, now the needles should be moved to a place that is more suitable; and do this until all of the fetus is delivered.
But if the hand is dislocated in front of the other, and it is not possible to reduce it due to its contraction, then wrap a cloth around it; and subsequently extract it until it is completely dislodged, and either incise it at the joint of the hand or at the elbow.
And do the same for the other hand and both feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or it happens that contraction occurs during extraction, or it is in the head of the tightly fitting water, then it is necessary to insert between the fingers and split the head and extract the water, or to expose it with an instrument called “alundat,” the shape of which will be among the forms of other instruments in the chapter that follows this one.
It seems as if it were the case that the bone is of great size by nature: and now it must be that incised part of the bone is bound up, or that it takes hold of that part of the bone in such a way as we have said; and then remove from the bones with the forceps.
And if the head is compressed and constricted behind the clavicle, then it must be split so that the moisture which is in the sinews may be effused.
But if it is not complicated, nor does it compress the head, then you must incise the head as soon as it is possible for you.
And if the lower part of the abdomen is abscessed or is in a state of dropsy, then it must be that you perforate that area through which flows the moisture that is there.
But if the substance is on the upper intestines, then the attraction of it is slight and the direction of it toward the mouth of the stomach is slight.
But if it is constricted behind the abdomen and the sinews, then it must be that you extract that part of the cloth which you have wrapped around your hand, and that you cut open the abdomen and the sinews through which flows what is within them.
But if the other members remain still and the head turns, then place your left hand in between; but if the mouth of the stomach is open, then insert your hand into the esophagus, and search for the head there, and pull it with those mentioned into the mouth of the stomach, and then insert one or two fingers, and pull it with them.
And if the mouth of the stomach is constricted by an abscess that has occurred to you, then it must be that it not be troubled over it; instead, it must be that you administer effusion of moist fatty substances. Apply plasters; and follow with waters that soften and moisten.
And if the condition is on the side, then as soon as possible, puncture it; and apply what we have said about treating it when it is live.
And if that is not possible, then cut it into pieces and extract it.
And it is necessary not to leave any cause inside for the second one.
And when you have completed your entire operation, then apply what we have said about treating abscesses for those in which such major issues occur.
And if there is bleeding, then bathe in astringent waters and proceed with the other treatment.
And I have seen a woman in whose womb there was a dead fetus, and above that focus another pregnancy, and above the other focus the fetus was also dead. It happened to one who had an abscess near their navel for a long time; it became swollen inside and the area around it grew soft, and they began to sweat.
And I was called to treat them, and treated them for a long time, but the condition did not improve.
Then I applied plasters with strong adhesive properties to them and extracted one bone from them, and after several days, I extracted another bone; and I was very surprised, as there were no bones in the abdomen.
Then I thought that they were the bones of the dead fetus.
Then, having diagnosed the condition, extract from it the bones that are present in its many parts, and since you have been observing this in such a manner for a long time, correct one by little bit.
And I do not consider it strange, since in this lies the science and helpers for what the physician uses his hand to administer in treatment.

And these two attachments should be inserted into the mouth of the main one so that it can be opened by them when you turn the two prescribed spirals.

Of the extraction of the second [incisor].
When the second [incisor] is retained during childbirth, you should command the patient to hold their breath, and then induce them to sneeze under certain conditions. Place your hand over the mouth and nose of the patient, and if it comes out this way, it is good.
And if not, then take a thread and pierce it through the covering at that spot, placing it near the mouth of the mother along with herbs such as chamomile, rue, anise, camomilla, Arabic scammony, cinnamon, and saffron. All of these herbs or some of them should be submerged in water with the thread, and then place it over the fire.
And then place a reed tube in the mouth of the mother, and hold it there until the vapor reaches the cavity of the mother.
And then induce her to sneeze as we have said, and the second [incisor] will be quickly expelled.
But if this treatment does not immediately produce results and it is not expelled, then command the midwife to immerse her left hand in sesame oil or masticate almonds, and then insert it into the woman's cavity and search for the second [incisor] with it. Once you have found it, gently extract it bit by bit. If it is stuck near the gums of the mother, then insert your hand as we have advised, and when you find the second [incisor], carefully remove it little by little so that the mother does not fall due to violent attraction. Take care that it is turned to the right and left with ease.
And if the opening of the mother is constricted, we have already recommended treatment through sneezing and medication of the ulcer with herbs.
But if it is not expelled by all the causes that we have recommended, then guard against pressing on it repeatedly with abstraction. Instead, what has been extruded to the opening of the mother should be managed with the substance of the woman, and subsequently crista-lyzed with an application of enguent and tetrafarmacon; for this will cause it to close over it within a few days and thus dissolve and be expelled.
But when you press on it, it causes severe pain in the head and stomach, and this aggravates the illness; therefore, it is necessary to administer subfumigations and appropriate treatments for that.
But some of the Ancients already recommended subfumigation with nitre and dried figs.
Form of an instrument with which a woman is subfumigated due to the retention of menstruation and metrorrhagia:

Of the treatment of the child's unopened anus.
Some children are born with their anuses not open, which must be carefully and skillfully opened. Then it is the midwife's duty to open that opening with her finger; and if not, she should use a sharp probe, being careful not to touch the membrane.
And then apply a poultice of lint soaked in wine and oil; and afterwards, treat that area until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is blocked, then place a small tube there for several days, and remove it when the child wants to pass gas.
And it may happen that the anus becomes blocked due to the consolidation of an abscess or swelling; then it is necessary to incise that consolidation, and afterwards treat it as we have said regarding treatment and lead.
Of the treatment of fistulas that occur below.
Fistulas that occur in the lower part near the anus are usually nodular and thick, which appear close to the anus or in the area of one side.
And it is either one fistula or multiple.
And as that nodule becomes old and drains watery white or subtle pus, and some of these fistulas may penetrate deeply.
And those that penetrate deeply are known by what is discharged from them due to evacuation and swelling; and sometimes there is an improper discharge, and it is easily discerned that these are severe.
Of those fistulas, some extend into the space penetrating into the bladder, and into the shaft of the penis; and some penetrate into the joint of the thigh and into the root of the bone.
And in that which is known as a penetrating fistula into the anus and a non-penetrating one, you should insert your finger into the anus, and introduce a fine thread of wool or iron into the fistula when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in that, then introduce a fine thread of lead or horsehair into it until you feel the thread or hair with your finger.
And if you do not feel anything at all and there is no cause for evacuation such as worms or flatulence, as we have said, then you will know that it is non-penetrating, and now attend to its treatment.
And when the fistula is penetrating into the bladder or into the shaft of the penis, then the significance of that is to discharge urine from it and to prevent anything that the place may be consolidated.
But if it is penetrating into the joint of the thigh or into the bone and causes pain in the circumference, then the sign of that is that the thread passes through there where there is no tortuosity into the bone, and causes pain around the affected area; and you will have an opening permanently; and nothing works in its treatment, nor can it be consolidated by any means.
And in all those penetrating fistulas there is no complete cure, and the treatment of these is labor and futility for physicians, as this is well established.
In that which is not penetrating and not ancient, healing with iron is hoped for as I will tell you, and this which I say is proper.
And it is that the patient should lie between your thighs with their back raised and lift the flanks up inside them, so that the objects are inclined towards their belly, and immediately insert a probe of lead or iron if it is in the tortuosity of the fistula, until you know where the probe passes.
And if that which the patient feels is around the anus, then it is necessary for you to insert your finger into the anus.
And if you feel with your finger that the probe has already penetrated by itself, or what you feel between your finger and that sinus or flesh, then you certainly know that it is penetrating; and then do not work on that one, for there is no healing in that one, as we have said.
But some might say that it heals in some men slowly.
And regarding the treatment through which help is hoped for in him, you should ignite a cautery subtly according to the size of the fistula, as has been previously described, and insert that heated instrument into the fistula until it reaches around the anus; and then repeat this two or three times until you know that all those inflamed tissues around the fistula are constricted, so that they resemble the quill of a bird.
And then treat that with bandages wrapped in butter until all those constricted tissues heal, and subsequently treat it with medicines that promote and consolidate the flesh. If it heals, that is good; but if not, then it will not be healed by any other treatment in any time.

On the treatment of abscesses and fistulas.

And truly, a fistula is a soft, hard, white lump in which there is no pain, and it is tender just as the sore of a bird's spur is tender; and for this reason it is called a curved ulcer, that is, a sore.
