On the treatment of the sinuses and the quality of enemas with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in some fleshy member and the space of the abscess is extended inward to form a collection of pus, and it is then ruptured and perforated, allowing the pus to be discharged, leaving the place empty as if it were a vessel, and the membrane that covers it remains thin like cloth and has not reached advanced corruption nor affected the bone or nerves or ligaments, it is called a sinus, and it is not named a fistula.
However, if the space extends inward such that corruption affects some member, it is then called a fistula and not a sinus.
The treatment for it is to incise that membrane completely as it is rolled up, especially when it is thin like cloth, and ensure that you do not adhere to the place due to its corruption.
However, if you hope that the membrane will adhere and that the corruption has not reached advanced stages, and there is a passage in the membrane, then you should treat it with an enema.
And when you consider the sinus, if it is large and the arm with which you work has a foul odor, then treat it with an Egyptian medicine consisting of oil, vinegar, and rose petals in equal parts, mixed together in a vessel and boiled over fire until the medicine thickens to the consistency of honey.
Take the water that is necessary and dissolve some of it with honey; then administer an enema with it, and stretch the opening, leaving the medicine inside for the quantity of two hours.
Then extract that with care; do this for several days until the sinus is cleansed and the pus is removed.
And sometimes an enema is administered with ash water when this medicine is not available to you; then take the ashes of wheat, put them over the water, and afterward strain it and administer that with it until you are confident that the sinus has been treated properly.
And if the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the ash water, then administer that with water and honey mixed three times, and also administer it with vinegar and honey mixed, because the property of honey is to cleanse and wash, and the vinegar makes the sinus adhere, especially when the vinegar has an abundance of astringency and dryness.
And as you do this for several days and become confident that the sinus is cleaned, then administer an enema with water that promotes flesh growth in it.
And it is that you take diaphoretic herbs and dissolve them in rose oil and astringent vinegar; or you administer an enema with various medicines and other herbs that have the properties I have already written about in the chapter on herbs.
And if the opening of the sinus is narrow and does not allow the enema tube to enter, then gradually widen it with a small instrument, and pause with a warm cloth wrapped in Egyptian or basilicon herb until it is widened.
If the opening is wide, then you should sew together the edges with a suture and leave enough space for the injection to enter.
And if the opening through which the arm is drawn out is raised toward the upper parts, then place it in the lowest position where the pus can drain and the arm can be moved downward, for when the arm is restrained at the depth of a bend, flesh grows around it.
And if it is not possible to place the bend in the proper position relative to the lowest pus, then make an opening that allows you to prepare the limb so that the arm can be moved easily according to your arrangement and without any hindrance to the movement of the arm.
But the cloth that is over the opening of the bend should be removed at all times so that you can adjust what is needed for the arm. And so that you may know whether what comes from the arm is much or little, or is digested or undigested.
In this way you should consider the location of that sinus, whether the patient feels pain there or not, and whether it is in an abscess.
And as your treatment is prolonged in this manner, and you see that the matter which is placed over the orifice of the sinus adheres, or begins to adhere, then you must loosen it or do so every third day, and when you loosen the plaster, then change the cloth and renew the application.
But if the sinus opens into a space where there is thin pus, then do not despair of its adherence, for healing will soon follow, because some people take longer to generate flesh in their plasters, and others are the opposite.
And if after many days the pus from the sinus is thick and undigested, then you know that the sinus does not adhere; and if the adherence of the sinus is delayed and becomes prolonged, then adjust to a drying effect in your medications, and if the medicines are humid by nature and in their virtue, make them dry as well when they are adjusted to an excess of egg white addition.
And the most important thing in curing this is to take a dry powder and dissolve it in rose oil, and apply it over an old dressing in its substance, and use it as needed.
And it appears in this action, when there is no pus, to take dry powder and mix it with honey that has been cooked until thickened, and use it; or you can take myrrh, aloes, and cumin, grind them all together, and sprinkle the powder over honey that has been heated on the fire, then apply this mixture onto a cloth and apply it to the sinus; or mix the dry powder with the honey that you have cooked until thickened, sprinkle it over a cloth, and apply it.

Of the extraction of the leg vein.
This vein is generated in the thighs in warm lands such as in Al-Ategi in Arab territory and in the warm and dry lands of India where no grass or grain grows.
And this vein is generated in other parts of the body besides the thighs.
And its generation is from putrefaction that occurs below the skin, just as serpents, worms, and scabies are generated within the body between the skin and the flesh.

And when the extremity of it appears, then you should wrap around it a piece of lead weighing one drachma out of two drachmas, and secure that area with the lead hanging from the thigh.
And so many times when you find some cause for parts outward, wrap it in lead and seal that. And if it is elongated, then you cut a part of it and wrap the remainder; and do not cut that which is rotten until it has come out, for if you cut that which is contracted it will enter into the flesh and cause an abscess and putrefaction in the place and a bad ulceration.
For it must be handled gently and extracted little by little until it is expelled from the rear of it and no cause remains in the body.
And of this vein it extends in some men to its length of five palms and I have heard that one reached twenty palms.

On the treatment of nasal illness.
This illness is called "nafir" in our land, and it is a pain that occurs in certain members of the body, and then it changes from one member to another.
And I have already seen that as I will now recount to you. I went to see a sick woman in a village and uncovered her arm. I saw a small inflammation in the vein called funis on the arm. After it was lanced for about an hour, I observed that this swelling moved just as vermin move upwards, but more quickly than is usual, as if it were living silver moving from place to place.
Then the pain moved from that location to the muscle, and it stayed there for about an hour.
And then it quickly moved to the other side of the body and reached the other arm.
And then you told me that it was moving throughout her entire body as I observed; and I was greatly amazed by its movement and the speed with which it moved from one limb to another. And I had seen something like this before in my own patients in that manner, but I have never seen pain being transferred from one limb to another so distinctly. But I did not see this in my patients as I did in the woman.
And I did not think this could happen except because the woman was of weak constitution, with thin limbs and exposed veins, in which this shifting airiness appeared to her senses.
And it is necessary that this does not appear in those who are robust and have well-filled veins and strong limbs.
And when you want to treat it and the patient feels that pain, immediately apply pressure on that area firmly and quickly so that the trapped airiness can be released, and cauterize the location.
If you do not use them [the instruments] to open the bowels, then cure that with evacuation of the body and with what expels windiness, and treat those with stinking pills and with Serapion pills and similar remedies.
Of the extraction of thorns.
Thorns are varied according to their species and according to the parts of the body where they lodge, for their diversity is that some have large points, others small, concave or blunt; and some solid. And there are those with three angles, and those which resemble tongues; and those which are like splinters.
And the signs of the brain when a thorn is lodged in it and penetrates the bone, and exits from the cephalus which is above the brain, are due to violent soda (possibly referring to an intense reaction or condition), scotomia, vertigo, rigidity in the eyes, inflammation and redness of the tongue, spasms, confusion of understanding, vomiting of bile, and often speech is lost and the voice destroyed. From the place of the wound, white moisture similar to powder oozes out, and it resembles the consistency of flesh.
And so if I have shown you these signs, then you should refrain from treatment and the extraction of the arrow unless it is outside.
Mark the signs of the arrow when it enters the heart, so that it is near the left nipple, and feel where it is lodged in a hard part, not a soft one; and lightly the movement of the arrow resembles the movement of the pulse, and there flows black blood from the wound, and coldness affects the extremities, and sweat turns cold, and fainting occurs; and thus you know that death will come very soon.
And the signs when the arrow has pierced the lung are frothy blood from the wound, and the veins near the neck become swollen, and the complexion changes, and there is great difficulty in breathing, and a craving for fresh air occurs.
And if the arrow hits the membrane that is in the thigh, and the pain then is near the small ribs, and it causes great distress with severe pain and sighing, and there is no strength left in all the limbs.
And if the arrow hits the stomach, then very lightly the wound vomits some undigested food.
And if the arrow hits the abdomen and is lodged there, the wound may emit some intestine, or membrane, or bowel that is ruptured, and there is no hope for recovery from the extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow hits the bladder and urine comes out from it, and some cause pain spreads to the surrounding areas, and there is severe pain over the wound, then you know that it is fatal.

On phlebotomy of the veins.
The veins for phlebotomy of which one usually performs phlebotomy are thirty, of which sixteen are here: two pulsating veins which are near the ears, called alhasisain; two that appear in the temples; and two that are in the tear ducts of both eyes that are called aspicientz; and the vein prepared in the middle part of the forehead; and the vein at the tip of the nose; and two glands that are on the neck; and two veins that are in the upper lip; and two veins that are in the lower lip; and these are the veins which are called algelic; and there are two veins below the tongue. The veins that are phlebotomized in the arms are five; of those is one cephalic, and it is called the wild laurel leaf vein by the people; and they call this "the head vein," and it is a median vein, and it consists of a branch from the basilic and another branch from the cephalic; and they name that one among the people as "the vein of the whole body"; and basilic is the vein which lies on the domestic arm, and it is called "cilium," and the common people call this "the abdominal vein"; and the brachial vein, and it lies over the humerus, and in it phlebotomy is performed, and it appears prominently above the wrist by a clear sign; and salvatella, and it is the vein which lies between the thumb and the next finger, and these are on both sides.
And in the thigh and the groin there are three veins; one of them is behind the curve of the wild laurel leaf vein; and the second is sophena, and its location is behind the domestic thigh groin; and the sciatic vein, and its location is behind the wild thigh groin; and in the groin there are other veins similar to those.
The treatment of veins near the ears is for catarrhs, emigrations, and old ulcers on the head.

And so the phlebotomy is finished.
But if the emission of blood is prohibited; then you immerse the foot in hot water and let the blood flow in that one until it is evacuated.
And if the phlebotomist errs on the first attempt, then repeat the phlebotomy above it a little higher, for that location is more effective; and do not fear making this mistake, but be careful of the nerves as we have said.
And do the same for the phlebotomy of the other foot equally.
And for the sciatic vein, the location is as we have said behind the heel below the malleolus of the leg.

And immediately loosen the bandage and place on the site of the phlebotomy cotton and tighten the site, for it is quickly healed.
Of venotomy and of the qualities of using those.
Venotomies are usually made from horns, or from ears, or from veins.

First chapter.
A summary and collection of the essentials concerning fractures of bones, from which a premise is necessary before we begin to recall the parts of fractured limbs and their various treatments. Therefore, let us proceed with the prologue of this chapter by summarizing the essence of the discourse; and the chapters are such that at the beginning it is necessary for the practitioner to pause and reflect on the truth of these matters; thus for you and those who wish to know or learn this noble art from others.
And thus I say that when it happens to someone to suffer a fracture, dislocation, torsion, or fall, at that moment it is necessary for you to consider at the beginning whether to perform venesection or bandage them, or both simultaneously, unless some cause prohibits this, such as weakness of constitution, or if the person who has suffered is an infant, elderly and infirm, or if it is a time of extreme heat or intense cold.
And you should be content with food such as fresh herbs and meat from birds and small animals; we prohibit fatty meats and foods that fill the veins with blood, until you are sure of a safe recovery from abscesses, and if you do not fear an effusion of matter to the limb.
And now return the patient to their original regimen, which they were accustomed to follow.
And when the broken bone begins to be restored, now it is necessary for the patient to be fed with nutritious foods that are moist and heavy, in which there is viscosity, such as food made from cooked wheat with water, rice, peas, beans, fresh poultry, and similar foods, and with this regimen the healing of the fracture occurs more easily and better.
And know that the bones in children as well as in the first stage of life and in the elderly cannot be joined and consolidated according to their nature by the drying up of the members.
However, nature adjusts and applies upon the broken bone from every part a cause similar to glue, in which there is a thickness that adheres and strengthens it as they are conjoined together, until they come to an end of strength and firmness, just as fire at the beginning, without any cause prohibiting them from their works.
And for this reason it is necessary that the patient abstain from foods that are heavy and viscous and gross, as we have said.
And know that the species of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the members, for the fracture of the bone of the skull is different from the fracture of the bone of the head; and the fracture of the bone of the foot is different from the fracture of the bone of the hand; and similarly all other members differ among themselves.
And I wish to give a recapitulation of every species of fracture, explained in its own distinct chapter.
The diversity of the fracture itself. And the species of fractures of the bone differ in themselves; for one type of curved fracture is different from those that occur in the stars; and some are made according to the length of the bone: and such are the fractures of the shaft, splinters, and fresh and old fractures, just as it is done in the pen when it is prepared to write, and there is a fracture by impact and disruption in the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
From the entire specialty comes the origin of a torment proper in the restoration of what will follow as a reminder of those that are distinct in their places.
Describe the sign of a bone fracture. And by this it is known that there is a bone fracture: there is a twisting or deformation of the bone, and an elevation or appearance of it to the touch, and pain when you press on it with your hand.
And therefore, when there is no visible deformation or pain, and upon gentle touch of the bone with some movement, the patient does not experience great pain, then there is no fracture here.
There might be a slight deformation or small fissure, so do not press on it with your hand and by compression from all sides; instead, apply medicine to it as is mentioned nearby the time when it is appropriate; and it may also be necessary for a slight restriction.
And know that the place where it is curved and not split into two or more as might occur during trauma, as described in its entirety by comparison with other cases, then you should begin from your time to correct it, provided that an abscess does not form around it.
And if an abscess forms around it, then leave it alone for several days until the abscess subsides; afterward, treat it according to each method that you can for lightness and healing.
And know that the restoration and alignment of it are more difficult than for the bone in which trauma has occurred.
And then strengthen it according to what will follow as a reminder of the next steps.
If you are in the bone and struggle, now it is necessary to extend this broken limb from two parts, if it is the hand or the foot; or with your hand if the member is small; or with two strings or with one string and the hand.
And be your position at which you place the limb in a manner equal to its natural figure, while the parts are extended of the broken member, now it forces you that you return those addictions to their origin and according to each way that you can in their places.
And be careful according to your strength that no harm comes to the patient from your operation or injury; and force yourself according to your ability to apply one bone to its companion bone according to their best forms.
And it is necessary that at that opportune moment you touch with your hand that which needs attention, and if there are any divergent causes, correct that and equalize it according to the measure of your skill.
And avoid excessive extension and compression, just as some foolish surgeons do, who through their carelessness cause abscesses to occur due to their operation, or cause contraction in the limb as we have seen happen due to their careless operations.
And the quality of the binding of the broken limb is what I recommend; so that you know that broken limbs vary in their smallness and greatness and in the form of those.
And for those that are small, such as the arm and the fingers and similar parts, now it is necessary that the wrapping be of soft fine cloth.
But for those that are large, such as the thigh or the chest, it is necessary that the wrapping be leather and hard, because the leather binding secures the large limb firmly from all sides in equal compression, so that nothing enters into it.
And immediately after you begin, immediately after your evacuation of the effusion, pause conveniently for this, on the place of that fracture with three wrappings or four as the limb deserves; and strengthen it with your hand a little over the bandage.
And then apply to the upper part of the place of the fracture, and tighten more so that your binding does not slip from the place of the fracture. And then extend the wrapping beyond the place of the fracture a little, and gradually loosen the binding until you have covered most of the area.
And then take another bandage and wrap that broken area again with the wrapping, and then apply to the lower part of the wrappings below the fracture; and let your operation be in the tightening of the binding and in the loosening of it as we have said about the first wrapping above.
And then pause between the wrappings to place a piece of soft linen or cloth over the curvature of the fracture if there is any curvature; and if not, do not pause for any reason.

Method of a plaster made with bran for the restoration of fractures, suitable for solid bones and mostly for women and children, as it does not attract heat or rubbing: take ground meal powder, which is the residue that sticks to the walls of the mill from the movement of the grinding stones.
And prepare that as it is, but sieve it with egg white, and make the application neither too thick nor too thin; and use it accordingly.
Another method of a plaster for restoring fractures, dislocations, and twists: recipe using asafetida, lapidani, acacia, angelica, and migrah, which is the root of wild millet, each one drachm; myrrh and aloes each five drachms; colocynth two drachms; all should be powdered and sieved and mixed with aqueous colocynth or egg white if the condition of the injury is inflamed; and then use this plaster, for it has a good composition and quickly restores broken bones, and suits men.
Another method of a plaster to apply for fractures and twists: recipe using migrah and the bark of white mulberry, each one drachm; myrrh and aloes each five drachms; acacia six drachms; Armenian bole two drachms.
All should be powdered in a mortar with a crushing stone, and then sieved, and mixed with water or egg white and applied.
Method of applying a plaster to joints and bones that have been dislocated from their places, and if pain occurs in them or for cracks and fractures: take clean wool and immerse it in sour wine and warm oil and apply it to the site.
In this plaster, there is no virtue for restoration, for abscesses, cauterization, and the proper expulsion of pains.
Another method of a plaster that restores a broken bone: take leaves of the fig tree and leaves of wild poppy; crush them all and make a plaster from them.
Method of a quick plaster to be used after the restoration of a bone fracture and when you want resolution of remaining abscess: take bark of elder, chamomile flowers, and violet flowers, and flour of rye, in equal parts; grind all together and cook with wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
But if it is inflamed, now prepare that with cold cilantro water or with water and vinegar.
Another method of a stronger plaster for resolution than the first one, when a hard abscess occurs after the restoration of the limb: take bark of elder and seeds of linden, hawthorn, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, in equal parts; grind all together and cook with sage water or with warm water or with wine, whichever suits the condition of the limb and according to its rest period.
However, Hippocrates does not mention in his book that it should be applied to the broken limb after its restoration except for a cerote made of wax and oil, and nothing else; he recommends that it be better midway between thickness and fineness.

Method of making a poultice for injury and pain: recipe calls for egg white, linseed, human hair or bird feather, and other ingredients, salt, in equal parts; they should be ground, sifted, mixed, and made into a poultice.
The quantity according to which it is appropriate for the bandage to remain and should be loosened is that you consider whether there has been no accident of pain or swelling, nor is the bone broken or moved from its place; then do not loosen it prematurely for several days. And if there occurs severe pain, swelling, or inflammation at the site, then you should be cautious and loosen that bandage immediately, and remove the poultice from it.
And take a cloth and sponge the wound with fresh water, then soak that in clean water, and wash the area until the swelling subsides and the pain is alleviated; then let the limb rest for an hour; afterwards place over it pure wool soaked in sour wine and oil or rose oil, and bind it with bandages overnight until you are sure there is no abscess, and to allow the swelling of the limb to subside, and to soothe the pain.
And then repeat that gentle binding with small poultices and do not tighten it as you did with your first compression, and gently continue this until it is healed.
And if you see that the abscess and hardness and pain and swelling and all other symptoms have subsided completely, and you need a poultice and binding, then repeat that as you did at the beginning similarly.
And if no cause happens to the member of those which we have mentioned, then you do not undo it but rather after three or four or up to ten or seven days; and sometimes all this is left for twenty days according to what appears to you from the loosening of the limb, as we have said, until the fracture adheres and sets with the coagulation of the flesh over it.
And now adjust the bandage more tightly than your first bandaging, and tighten according to the swelling of the injured part, as was previously described.
But if you see the location of the fracture already drying and wasting more than is appropriate, then know that nourishment is not reaching it; therefore, apply tepid water compresses to it every time you loosen it; do this over three days; also loosen the bandage slightly, and through this operation nutrition will return to the limb, and it will be healed quickly.
But what those foolish restorers of the fractured limb do otherwise when it is not restored at the beginning as needed and is restored in a crooked manner, is an error according to their procedure, and great fear, and if they were judges, this would be greatly condemned by the Ancients in their books and rightly so.
And I have not seen any of them do this, which is why it is more reasonable that they should not do so.
Chapter Second: On fractures when they occur in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their figures are diverse, and the causes are distinct. Some fractures are caused by the impact of a hard object, and the impact breaks all the bone within its reach, which is behind the bone, just as it would in wood; thus, this type is called an ax blow. Or a sharp instrument may cut some part of the bone and separate the flesh from it without passing the incision to the back of it; and this kind of fracture is called absolute elevation.
Of the plague of these two fractures, one is great or small, and of the fracture that is comminuted or greenstick; and the cause of it is percussion by falling or striking upon a stone, and similar things.
And this fracture is penetrating, which already approaches the periosteum below the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the condition of these two fractures is either serious or minor.
And of the fracture that is delicate in the bone, in the fineness of the hair-like structure, and for this reason this particular type of fracture is named capillary.
And it is the fracture that is caused either by falling or by striking with a stone and similar actions, which causes some plate of bone to enter into the soft parts inside, and it occurs in the concave location just as it happens with the ribs when they are struck, causing their fragments to enter into the soft parts inside.
And this is more commonly done in the bones of living beings, just as it happens with the heads of children.
And for all these types of fractures, treatments are applied, which are methods or remedies separated just like stars. And I will return to the recollection of all this in its place.
And you should recognize all these types of fractures by detection on them and by inquiry into their symptoms and the discolored or corrupted flesh that is above them.
And the capillary fracture is recognized by this: it is exposed on the bone, and when cleaned, it appears black when examined.
In the treatment of fractures at the beginning consider the symptoms of the patient, and if you see by the symptoms that it signifies through manifestation great fear, as is vomiting of bile, and extension, and loss of understanding, and hoarseness, and syncope, and acute fever, and similar such symptoms in major dispositions, without doubt it is not healed.
And if you see uncommon symptoms that give hope of health, then you begin the treatment of it.
When the cause of the ailment has come to you at the beginning as it does with this ailment and this is in the days of winter, then it is necessary that you study the condition of the bone until the day fourteen according to all dispositions.
And if it is in the days of summer, then it is necessary that you expose the bone before the seventh day, because what is around the bone may become corrupted by inflammation, and for the symptoms we have recounted to occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the membrane covering the brain, and it is in small pieces and abraded, then you must excise the comminuted and abraded part, as I shall relate to you.
And it is that you shave the head of the patient who is ill, and uncover the bone according to custom and method as best as possible according to the shape of the injury, so that it is clear on the patient.
And if it happens to you that there is difficulty in detecting the bone, or bleeding or a boil requires attention, then you must deal with what is necessary, which is to apply bandages infused in wine and oiled cloth to the area, and let the injury rest until the boil subsides and the bleeding stops; and afterwards begin circumferential treatment around the bone and its removal.
And this is according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you incise the bone with an incisor of such a shape, and this is its form: And then apply another incisor, this one slightly wider than the previous one; and this is its form:
And then apply an even wider incisor than the second one.
And it is necessary to have various incisors, some of which are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than the others, and their tips should be sharp, made of iron or steel.
And use them with ease during percussion on the incisors so that you do not injure the head and cause pain to it.
And if the bone is strong and hard, it is necessary to drill around it before you apply the incisors with the chisels, which are called non-profound chisels.
And they are called this way because these do not penetrate the bone of the head to where it is near it, so that the chisel has a rounded tip or margin above which the bone beneath the head prevents from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take many of these chisels, each of which should suit the shape of each bone, so that you have present in all parts of the skull an end that is sharp, the quantity in length and breadth being according to the quantity of convexity of this part.
This is the form of three kinds of probes: The quality of piercing around the broken bone is that you place the probe on the bone and roll it about with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated; then change the probe to another spot, and continue this throughout the length, in proportion to the thickness of the rib or approximately so.
Then incise with the scalpel that which is between each segment of the bone, and make it as fine as possible towards the end for ease of movement, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either by hand or by some other means you have prepared for this purpose, such as probes and fine forceps.
And you must be careful that the scalpel does not touch any part of the skin.
And so when you have perforated the bone in a circle, and it is separated from the skin if it continues there, and you have extracted it, you should rasp and smooth that part where it meets the bone with an instrument similar to a scalpel, but one that is sharper and finer than other scalpels.
And if any part of the small bones remains and causes you difficulty, now take that with ease using the instruments at your disposal; then treat the wound with lint and dressings as we have said.
And the other method of operation is light and free from fear, which Guila calls and praises highly; this is the speech on it: It is necessary for you at the beginning to start uncovering the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most severe, until when you have uncovered that part and have placed below it the tip of this slow and steady scalpel, whose form is as follows:
This is that the lens-shaped part is solid and not incised for any cause, and the acute part is cut into two parts forward in length, as you see; that the lens-shaped part be fitted to the suture with the cutting surface acute on the bone.
And so strike upon the saw of a part with a small hammer so that you cut through all the bone with ease as it revolves, and be sure of the soft tissue that no accident happens in any way, and if the operator were the most foolish of all men and the least skilled among them; certainly, and even if he were asleep.
Now if there is any cause attached to the bone of the soft tissue in some places of the bone, it will cut that which is adjacent to the end of the lens-shaped saw, and deliver that which is with ease, for it is delivered without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the soft tissue, and what is abraded or remains of the bone is its surface, and it remains with roughness and small fragments, now you should scrape that roughness and eradicate those fragments with subtle razors for which you have prepared in various quantities so that you can administer them appropriately at any location and most correctly according to how your operation on that broken bone leads you and its shape.
And you should administer in your proportion at which you scrape the bone at the beginning with the broader of those razors, and then use progressively the finer ones, and do not cease doing this continuously until you reach administering the thinnest and finest of all of them.
But in each of the other small capillary fractures and subtle fractures, you should administer care according to what leads you towards correction.
It is for this reason that it is not surprising on all those who have studied in that art. And this about what we have written has been shown in large fractures.
And if the muscle is rectangular when you remove the bone, now cover that with cloth according to the quantity of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound; then take another folded or tripled cloth and immerse it in vinegar and rose oil, and place it on the first cloth, and do this as quickly as possible without pressing it so much that you injure the tissue; and then apply a linen bandage, but do not tighten it more than what is necessary to cover the wound.
And leave it with this treatment for one day or two until you are sure of the condition of the abscess; then untie it and afterward administer medicines that are suitable for excessive dryness, such as linseed poultice and flour of wheat, and powder of ashes and of aristology, and similar ones.
And it is that you should make these medicines into powder that is finely ground and applied to the wound, as they are dry; and generally, administer all medicine whose property is to cleanse without irritating.
And guard yourself according to your virtue that the wound in your treatment be clean; and it should not become infected or worsen; nor should you allow it to increase in virulence. For when it is set upon the muscle of the brain, it corrupts it and weakens it, and this leads to severe illness due to such malevolent harm.
And it affects the tissue of the head, as when the bone is cleansed and especially when the treatment is pressed too much, and there is blackness on the surface; and therefore consider now whether it leads to the illness we have mentioned, knowing that it is dangerous without any doubt.
And if the blackness does not come from the medicine that was applied above, but rather due to the virtue of that medicine which causes the blackness, then you should take one part honey and three parts rose oil, and with these ingredients moisten a cloth; then place that over the wound. And subsequently treat it with the spices appropriate for healing until it is healed.
Chapter III.
On the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except in two places above mostly, or one of those sometimes, because there are two bones there, as the lower part is cartilaginous and does not break, and what happens to it is only ulceration, tortuosity, and curvature.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then you should insert your little finger into the side of the nose and align that fracture with your middle finger from outside until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this with ease, and be careful that your operation does not cause pain to the patient.
And if the fracture is in the uppermost part of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, then you should use the tip of a probe with some thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you should proceed as described.
And attempt the restoration of it on the first day of the fracture if possible; and if not, then do so around the seventh or tenth day when the swelling has subsided.
Insert a piece of cloth between the edges of the nose if the fracture is in one part, or use two pieces if the fracture is in two parts, and let each piece be thick enough to fill the hollow of the nose.
Some of the ancient restorers say that the cloths should be dipped in boiled honey and changed every day, but I have not seen this done. However, it is advisable to dip the cloth in albumen of hens' eggs mixed with powdered sugar; then place the cloth inside until the bone is set and the cartilage has hardened.
And sometimes a piece of wax is inserted into the nose instead of cloth, wrapped in silk cloth; this should be done when the fracture of the nose is more severe and the patient is not prohibited from inspiration.
And this method is not necessary for you; if you wish, do it this way, and if you wish, use cloths.
And if another swelling occurs that needs to be cauterized, apply a plaster to the nose made of cerot or cotton dipped in vinegar and rose oil, or with diaquilon.
But if it does not require cauterization, then you should apply a plaster outside made of sesame flour and powdered coriander mixed with egg white; then place it over clean linen, and do not cover the nose with anything else.
But if the bones of the nose are fractured in small pieces or comminuted, then you should carefully cut around them and extract them with instruments that are suitable for this task; then close the gap, and treat it with substances that promote healing and consolidation, and use appropriate plasters.
And if it happens that a swelling occurs in the nose, at this time you must treat it with poultices and administer lead tubes within so that it may be completely healed.
Chapter IV.
On the restoration of the jaw when it is dislocated.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture does not cause swelling, observe at this time if the fracture is only on the surface and it is not broken into two parts and curved inward, then its diagnosis is easy.
And you must therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, insert your left thumb into the mouth of the patient; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert your right thumb; and press with it inward on the swelling of the fracture gently outward with your other hand from outside the bone; in this manner appropriately adjust it.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two parts, then you must administer extension of both parts according to straightness as much as possible until they are aligned.
And if it happens that there is concussion of the teeth with disruption, then strengthen those which you expect to break with a thread of gold or silver or silk; and immediately place over the broken jaw some cerate, and then place on it a doubled cloth, and place on it a large piece of linen properly made, or a pad of wax equal in length to the jaw; and then bandage it from above as you are prepared for binding it and as is convenient for its application, so that it is not destroyed.
And he commands the patient to use rest and tranquility, and to pause from liquid foods.
And if you see that the shape has already been altered in any way, then on the third day you should examine and loosen it; and afterward correct what has already been altered with powder of myrrh, egg white, or flour of linseed, after you have removed the cerot, and place over the emplastrum a cloth.
And then as it is attached and adjusted that emplastrum over it, and the position of the bone has not been altered, leave it, so do not loosen it until it is healed and the fracture is strengthened; for in many cases this fracture is cured in three weeks.
And if in another case an abscess occurs, then administer what we have mentioned many times for its reduction until it is healed, and let that abscess be brought to a head.
And if the fracture is with a splinter, then consider whether it is a piece of bone separated or stuck, and you should carefully remove those splinters with what is appropriate for removing them using the instruments.
And if the opening of the splinter is small, then enlarge it with a scalpel according to the amount necessary for your needs.
And then when you have removed those splinters, and if they cannot be brought to a head, close the opening of the splinter if it is large; and if not, place over it one of the emplastrums that are appropriate for it and promote healing there, until it is healed.
Chapter 4.
On the restoration of the collarbone when it is broken.
Most often the collarbone is broken at the part in front of the muscle, and that fracture occurs in one of three ways.
For it is either broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without any displacement; and the restoration of it is quick. Or it happens with some displacement, and its restoration is difficult; or it is such that the fracture involves an injury.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture involves an injury, is that you make two assistants present, so that one holds the patient's arm and supports the broken collarbone, and the other extends the neck towards that part.
And then adjust the fracture with the fingers so that it is set in the proper form, and there should be neither a small prominence nor a depression.
And if you need more extension, then place under the armpit of the injured side a pad made of cloth or wool, and its size should be according to your requirement; extend and elevate the collarbone and compress the pad with your hand so that you adjust the fracture as needed.
And if you cannot pull the end of the collarbone to the outer parts, so that it reaches the preon, then have the patient lie supine on their neck, and place under the muscle a pad about half as large.
And the assistant presses the muscle from below so that the bone of the collarbone is elevated to reach the preon, and then you correct the fracture by adjusting it with your fingers.
If you feel pain from the fork and its prongs, now you must cut into that area and extract those pains with ease.
And if it is stuck in the bone, now you must devise an incision with one of the instruments that you have prepared for this purpose, since you had placed below the fork the tool used for sawing; and this tool is made of wood or iron, and its shape is as follows:
And it resembles the bottom of a bowl, which is concave; and its width should be according to what you need from it, depending on the size of the bone or the narrowness of it.
But the length of it should be according to what is possible for the operation; it should have two ends as you see fit, and one end should be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound that you have incised when extracting the pain from the bone is wide, and you are sure it may become an abscess, now you must bring the two lips of the wound together with a suture.
And if the disruption is narrow, or you fear it may become an abscess, now you must cover the wound with patches and powders according to the amount of the wound's incision.
And if an abscess occurs, now you should apply a patch made of soft cloth and rose oil with vinegar, and place it over that area.
And do not let the splint rest upon the broken bone; and therefore it is necessary that you see the patient every day, so when the bandaging has softened or you see the splint already moved out of place, then you must correct this and tighten the bandage.
And place the patient's pillow on his neck, and put under it something to raise the head of the pillow slightly for the night; therefore, lift up the support from his side so that the collarbone is raised by the muscle elevation; or tie his arm to his neck.
And do not remove the bandage unless an abscess occurs in the vicinity, within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you see his face for this reason, and adjust it to the constriction and restore it until it is set and the collarbone fracture is healed.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the measure in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time.
Chapter VI.
On the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The blade is broken at the side and not in the middle, only the edges are fractured.
And when it is broken at that place — and it is broken in the middle — and it is only known by touch, then according to the figure of the fracture force the preparation and reduce it according to its natural shape in every way that is possible for you.
Here is the translation:
If you place the blade in one of the hollows on the back of the spoon, then wrap that hollow with a lion’s paw and subsequently press the blade evenly.
And then press from above with a metal rod of appropriate length and with sufficient force on the blade so that it does not move from its place.
And examine the ligature every day; and whenever it is softened, replace it and tighten it if the blade has moved from its place.
And place the ligature over the appropriate site.
And the spoon is restored in xx days or in .xxv days.
And when this number of days is complete, then you remove the ligature, and be sure that these are bones from which the beginning was not feared nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed from the place of the bone and place the chain below it, then you cut above it, remove it, and do as we have said regarding the fracture of the furcula or in case of a suppurating abscess if it occurs here.
Chapter VII.
On the restoration of a fractured foot.
The foot is sometimes broken in the middle and remains stable there; however, its ends move as cicatrization and comminution occur.
And when the middle of it is broken, the symptoms are that it inclines towards the inner parts and there is great pain, difficulty in movement, coughing, shortness of breath, and vomiting blood; and these symptoms are evident in the cavity within the broken bone and are not hidden from sensation.
For the restoration of it is that the patient lies on their back, and a bolster is placed under their shoulders; then press the muscles of it, and adjust the ribs of it with two fingers, and delicately do this in the operation of it and in the correction according to every possible way that is available for this, until the figure of the bone returns as required.
Then place on it the plaster and cotton, and place underneath a fine splint of hazel or willow or something similar in lightness, then wrap that in cloth; then delicately bind that above the broken bone without much pressure; and proceed with bandaging around the back several times, and tighten that with appropriate firmness; then consider the bandage at all times, and when it has softened, strengthen that.
And if necessity requires the removal of that due to corrosion or swelling, or pain, or an abscess that occurs in the area, then you should examine and loosen that, remove the plaster, and rectify what has occurred according to the method of treatment; and then reapply the plaster if it is necessary for this purpose; and adjust the firmness until it is healed.
Chapter VIII.
On the restoration of the ribs when they are broken.
Know that fractures of the ribs do not occur except in thick parts where they meet at the back; and from their extremities in front, those are not affected by such breaks because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from the sensation during the examination of it with the aforementioned methods.
And so the restoration should be such that it equalizes the fracture according to the mentioned method as far as possible, but let it fit as is necessary, and then apply a plaster and bind the broken bone with a splint if that is required.
And if the fracture of the ribs inclines towards the inner parts, it often causes severe pain and piercing, like the piercing that occurs in one who weeps, and because of this the bone puts on a membrane; and it leads to difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood; and this is difficult to cure.
And the Ancients were skilled in that with skillful words.
And what they say is that you should avoid foods for the patient that cause inflammation and gas, so that the belly does not swell and become extended, and thus the fracture is pushed outward.
But we should do this to ensure it does not lightly cause an abscess to form, if it does not happen already; and if it does happen, then this helps with that.
And some of them say that it should be stopped above the wind point and then sucked out with force; and this is similar to reasoning, but it is feared that the wind point will draw the superfluities to the location due to weakness.
And some of them say: the area should be covered with wool that has been submerged in hot oil, and then powders should be applied between the ribs so that it is filled evenly when wrapping it according to its roundness; and subsequently, the patient should be treated as if for pleurisy with food and medicine.
And if the patient is very constrained by pain which they cannot bear, and the bone has penetrated through the dressing with a puncture wound, we fear for the patient, at that time it is necessary to cut above the location, uncover the broken part, and then proceed with the instrument that preserves the continuity of the bone, the memory of which precedes, and that we should open the bone easily, and extract it.
And then we should adjust the two edges of the wound, if it is large, by stitching, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs internally, at that time use a poultice and apply powder in warmed oil on the area, and treat the patient with what is necessary for the internal abscess.
And let them be reclined on the side on which they sleep until they are healed.
Chapter IX.
On the restoration of fractures of the spine and the neck.
In some cases, fractures occur in the bones of the neck, and similarly in their healing, as well as in the spine of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if they will heal or not, at that time consider, for if you see their fingers already softened and numb and dead, and they cannot move those nor extend them nor strengthen them, and when you constrain those with your hands or pierce them with a needle and there is no pain in them, then you know that it is not healed, and in such conditions it is mortal.
And if they move those and feel constriction in the fingers when you constrain them and pierce them with a needle, then you know that the bone of the neck is safe and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if you see the vertebrae of the neck in such a condition and want to know whether it will heal or not, then consider the state of health.
Now if you see that he is already softened [paralyzed], and accidents befall him as they often do in such cases, and soon when he lies on his back, there is flatulence and involuntary evacuation, and sometimes he is arched backwards and wants to urinate but cannot, then you know that it is mortal, and henceforth do not labor in his cure.
But if none of those causes occur, the cause is better and more hopeful.
And his treatment is to apply cauterization to the abscess after placing over the protruding vertebra either olive oil or a mixture of heated oils; place this there several times during the day until the abscess is thoroughly reduced.
Now apply one of the comforting and stimulating plasters, and wrap it around that area; and instruct the patient to rest and remain calm, and not to sleep on the side where he feels pain until it is healed.
And if it happens that the healing of the bone is delayed or for some cause it is separated from it, then you should immobilize the joint and extract that bone; and subsequently close the two edges of the wound, with sutures if it is large; and afterwards treat it with one of the ointments that promote flesh growth until it is healed.
But if the end of the bone is broken at the joint, and it is the end of the limb, then you should insert your left hand into the armpit, and align the broken bone with the other arm as much as possible by second-degree reduction; and subsequently apply a plaster and bandage to it, if needed.
And if you find bleeding veins in there, then apply your hand on that, remove it, and treat the wound with the treatment that has been described until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
On the restoration of the broad area/wound.
The bones of the broad areas are often broken, and when they break, the fracture of them is only communicated at the extremities of those bones and they are split along their length and deviate to the sides; and it happens that the patient experiences pain in that location and puncture, and the limb is stunned when there is a fracture.
And the restoration of it is that you place your hand on it until it is in the position where the fracture aligns with its natural figure.
And if the fracture is at the extremity of it only, then you should set that according to what seems appropriate to you by equalizing it until it resembles its natural figure.
But if the fracture is in the long bone or one that has already deviated to the sides, then you should have the patient lie on their back until you are prepared for the restoration of that fracture. And when you have set that, apply a plaster over it, and then place a splint of wood or cane next to it, and bind it tightly with bandages so there is no risk of puncture from the fracture or movement of the splint.
And adjust the padding to fill that space until it conforms to equality with the bandaging, and instruct the patient to sleep on their back or side.
If an inflamed abscess occurs after cauterization, you should stop the extension of it and its restoration until the cauterized abscess is calmed, and proceed with what follows according to that which precedes; then return to the restoration of it, and strengthen it as is appropriate.
And if you encounter them in the bone or they are crushed, then you should treat them.
Chapter XI.
On the restoration of the fracture of the auxiliary bone.
The auxiliary is what is between the shoulder blade and the top of the shoulder.
And if it is fractured, then the restoration of it is in one of two ways, one of which is that you take a flat splint of moderate thickness according to this form:
And let them be tied at both ends with two bandages, and then it should be suspended from a high place.
And let the patient lie on a mattress; then place the injured arm, which is fractured, on the splint until it reaches the midpoint of its curvature.
And then suspend something heavy on that to cause it to extend downwards; and then the physician should keep the fracture in place with his hands until the fracture returns to its proper position.
And the other method is that the patient lies supine with their neck, and suspend the arm at the neck with bandages; then command two assistants, one of whom holds above the fracture with his hands, and the other holds below it; each should extend in their respective directions.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then tie one bandage over the fracture and another bandage above it, and each of the two assistants should pull on their respective sides.
And therefore, if the fracture is close to the muscle's extremity, then fold one half of the bandage over the button (citollic) and place the other half below the fracture, wrapping it around.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the joint, then fold the bandage over that place and around that joint.
Fold it in the same way with ease and effort, and as soon as it is folded appropriately and applied well, then tie it securely if no abscess occurs.
But if an abscess does occur, then loosen the bandage within seven days, and place over it wool soaked in vinegar and rose water until the abscess subsides, and then tie it securely.
And the method of securing it is to place a plaster over the fracture, and then wrap a wet cloth around the plaster; then adjust the arm with the aid of a support, and place its hand on its own muscle.
And place the cloth and secure it around the support so that the arm is in a fixed position, unless some cause related to the broken bone prohibits this.
And now use splints.
And it is that you place on the fracture the splint that is closest and strongest among the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints follow the break with an additional three fingers on all sides.
Upon this band place the ligature which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your ligature at the place of the fracture be placed tighter. And the further apart the fracture is, the smaller should be the ligature.
And thus, when you have positioned the bones and the ligature as we have said, at that time you do it.
And if you fear an abscess, then at that time loosen the ligature and the bandages on the seventh day as we have said.
And then consider the bandaging in all parts for three days so that no swelling or inflammation occurs at the site properly or due to excessive tightening of the ligature; therefore, adjust everything as we have said.
But if you are confident that no cause of this will occur, then do not loosen the ligature for three days or more.
And let the patient's position be lying on their back, and their arms be across their stomach.
And place a pad filled with wool on the helper, and at all times of the night or day ensure that the figure of the broken limb is not loose or dissolved, or that the bandaging is too relaxed, so adjust this according to your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we mentioned earlier, such that the food is light at first until the bandaging begins; then it should be gradually made heavier.
As to the custom of the assistant and of the dressing of the wound, they should be tight for forty days; and then it is necessary that they be loosened and a bath be administered and medicine be applied with those remedies which correct it.
And if the fracture is rubbing against itself, then you do not loosen it from that bandage and the splints for one day or even two months.
Chapter XII.
On the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called the ulna and radius; one of which is small, and that is the one attached to the wrist, and the other is larger and is situated below the smaller one, towards the lower part.
And if the larger bone or the smaller one is broken separately, or both together.
And when the smaller bone is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is quicker.
And when the bone is broken below, the fracture is severe, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller one above, then it is appropriate for the physician, during its restoration, to allow a slight extension with ease until it is set straight.
But if the larger bone is the one that is broken, then it is necessary to allow more vigorous extension.
And if the two bones are cut together, then at this time you must pause the cutting until pus is stronger.
And it is necessary to pause with the hand in the position for recovery and extension laid out on a pillow, with the wrist above all other parts, so that all others are lower than the mentioned auricular bone, which should be lower than the rest, and the patient should lie on a tilted bed.
And let there be a pillow in front of him at an elevation or straight ahead, so that the patient does not have to strain.
And let one attendant extend the arm from below with his hand or bandage, and another attendant extend it similarly from above.
And let the physician then insert the bone into its place as best as possible according to the proper figure.
And if in the process of setting the bone you encounter difficulty, then at this time you must use all your skill to reduce every part to its proper place.
And if a distinct difficulty appears that makes the condition worse and does not give you hope for recovery, then at this time you should act on it and remove that part according to the method we have mentioned above.
But if the fracture is accompanied by inflammation, I have already devoted a separate chapter to this, and thus follow the treatment described there.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your recovery, then you should line a cloth with cerot made from rosined oil and white wax, which is medium between thickness and subtlety, and apply a bandage over it delicately so that the abscess can drain; and then remove the cerot and apply a plaster prepared from powdered herbs mixed with egg white, and subsequently apply strips of cloth; and let the strip placed over that fracture be wider and stronger.
And know that the number of stars on the arm can be seen in major dispositions, or whether the fracture is in one fossa or in two fosse simultaneously.
And appropriately apply pressure to the site of the fracture firmly and vigorously, and when you will loosen the bandage above the fracture or below it, apply the pressure a little more gently according to how it was preceded by the recollection of it at the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that you wrap around the fracture should be soft and moistened cloths, not hard or rough, and the thread with which it is bound should be of medium thickness between fine and thick, as we have recommended.
And consider the limb and the bandaging after some days. And if any cause occurs for which correction is necessary, such as swelling that happens in the limb, then you should soak the limb in hot water until that swelling subsides.
And let the limb not be constrained until it rests, and then reapply the bandaging.
And if the bandaging has already been loosened and the bone is already repositioned as it should be, then correct everything with your skill.
And consider whether nutrition is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive bandaging, for then you should loosen it slightly and leave it for several days until nutrition reaches it, and then rebind it.
But if the patient does not experience any of the causes we have mentioned, then it is not necessary to loosen it for about 20 days or thereabouts.
And so the hand of the sick person is suspended by its upper part for active movement, and make them sleep on their back.
And know that this fracture is restored in xxx days or in xxxii days, or at the lightest in xxviii days, and all of this depends on the condition of the tissues and the strength of the disposition.
Chapter XIII.
On the restoration of a fracture of the bone in the hand and of those mentioned.
Know that to the bone in the hand and to the knuckles of the said fingers, when they are fractured, and such accidents do not happen to them but after many days.
And when fractures or injuries occur to the hand, it is then necessary for the patient to lie flat and in front of them place the injured hand on that surface extended.
And then the attendant should extend the broken bones and the physician should ensure that they are properly set in place.
And then it is necessary to apply a plaster and wool, unless an abscess occurs which requires cauterization; and afterwards place a splint above according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped that in linen cloth.
But if the fracture is in the parts inside the palm, then make it appear like a sphere of cloth, and command the patient to hold that with their fractured palm; afterwards wrap it tightly with cloth.
And the splint should be made of wood wrapped in cloth that is raised, so that the splint fits against the palm; and wrap it as needed.
If the fracture is in the upper parts, at that time place one splint above and another below the injured part in the palm, so that it may be open and straight; then bind it securely, as if you were wrapping the hand with bandages between the said splints.
And if it happens that the fracture occurs in one of the bones of the said fingers, at that time if the finger is small, place it parallel as needed and then secure it to the palm; and if you wish, place a small straight splint so that the fracture is elevated and not compressed.
If the fracture is in other fingers, such as in the middle finger, or in the index, or in the ring finger, at that time place it parallel and bind it to the affected finger; or bind all of them continuously, which is better; or place a small splint over it, as we have said for the toes.
And consider, in your treatment, and subsequently, the disposition of abscesses, cauterization, and opposition—during your operation—to what is needed when any cause of these occurs, as repeatedly narrated.
Chapter XIV.
On the treatment of a fracture of the collarbone.
The collarbone, which is often fractured, becomes apparent by touch according to how it has shifted in the affected area and to the most prominent part.
And the treatment of it is done in such a way that one bandage is wrapped above the fracture and another below the fracture, and the patient lies prone on their belly and face.
And so the surgeon should extend the bandage throughout its application according to the quality, when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone.
But if it extends towards the root of the spine, then at this point the bandage should extend from the hand to the part above the elbow, so that its extension ends there, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture extends towards the elbow, then at this point the bandage should extend from the elbow, and its extension should be to the parts below.
And so the surgeon aligns the bone with both hands but ensures it is reduced according to its natural appearance, and the bone is joined by good union; then it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, unless an abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess occurs, then you should leave it for several days until the abscess has subsided, and then return to your treatment.
The bandaging should be done as follows: it is necessary to wrap a hard bandage over the fracture, and fold it two or three times, and press out the excess; then wrap the entire area so that it reaches the wrist and the root of the spine.
And insert a long strip of cloth between the skin and the bandage near the elbow on the lower parts, and let the ends of the cloth extend from the most prominent part of both sides, and then roll over the limb any excess of the bandage.
And then apply splints to the skin at the location of the fracture, and place one strip over the bone of the limb.
And fill the void between the bowl and the container with soft cloths, so that the constriction may clot.
And then begin the firm binding of the middle over the site of the fracture with three or four layers, and as much as you distance yourself from the ligature of the place of the fracture, your binding should be lighter and softer.
And then extend the two ends of the bandage downward to the joints below until it reaches the nail of the toe, and tightly bind both ends of the bandages from the other side so that the ligature is not moved from its place.
And then place the binding over it in such a way that corrosion, abscess, swelling, or similar conditions do not occur to the limb; and if any cause of those should arise, immediately undo it and correct what has occurred as soon as possible, as we have said before.
But if in the bone there is a piece of bone that needs to be placed, then it is necessary for you to align it as much as possible; and if not, bandage over it and move it, and treat with what precedes the memory until it is healed.
And in some cases, this fracture of the bone is tightly bound with something that fits around it, as we have said regarding the assistant and the arm.
But in the restoration of the limb done in such a way, no improper limpness should occur; and if it is restored alone or with another bandage applied, then it is necessary that the person limps permanently.
And know that the wound is healed in forty days or a few months, and all of this depends on the diversity of temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter XV.
On the restoration of the fracture of the lower jawbone.
Know that the fracture of the lower jawbone usually occurs here, but such accidents rarely happen.
But if such a fracture does occur, it is usually just a crack or a contusion in its parts, and this is either one pain or several pains, and all of this depends on the sensation.
And the restoration of it is such that what is broken in its parts must be equalized, and this should be joined and conjoined as much as possible by adjustment, ease, and fabrication.
And then apply a plaster and place it over that round bandage if you need one; and bind it above with a bandage as is appropriate.
And then consider all the conditions we have recommended for other fractures, as well as abscesses and similar treatments that oppose any incident so that it is appropriate for healing until it is healed.
Chapter XVI.
On the restoration of the fracture of the skull.
Know that in the skull there are two bones of which one is thick, and it is named the skull bone; and the other is thin, and it is named the parietal bone.
And it happens with some of the types of fracture that happen to both bones of the arm, and by this restoration is made as with the restoration of the arm equally, and the operation of it is one.
And if you break both bones together, the curvature is distributed throughout all parts.
And if the more delicate bone is broken, the curvature is converted to the forward parts.
And if the thicker bone is broken, it affects that below it, and it is of those causes that are not to your liking.
And immediately administer extension and alignment of the fragments according to that proportion.
But it is necessary, if the fracture of the curvature is jagged with multiple stages, that the extension be lesser and more gradual, and proceed with your skill.
And in the curvature there is some cause for operation on the arm, and it is when you align the fragments and complete all of the main operation, then you will take pieces of wood from a tree that grows below the causes which are planted in rows, and made similarly from palm branches; choose those with slight roughness, and not too thick, and their length according to the length of the curvature, and about an inch within the most distant.
And then wrap around each one a cloth in two layers according to the length of it, and place one at the side of the curvature on one side and the other on the opposite side, and they should be about an inch above the lowest part of the body.
Apply the dressing to the two flaps in three places at each extremity and in the middle place, for in this structure the scabbard is forbidden to incline to the right and left, and it stands in a good position.
And now a piece of wood has been administered according to the length of the scabbard; and let it be placed where the scabbard rests so that it may preserve its range of motion.
And especially do this in the case of a fracture, and when it is properly a contusion.
And each day consider the scabbard and study it based on previous studies, and you must guard it from abscesses and inflammation or other similar causes; and when any such cause occurs, then act appropriately to address it until it is healed.
Chapter XVII.
Of the fracture of the shinbone and of the bones of the foot and of the aforementioned.
A fracture does not often occur in the shinbone; but fractures frequently occur in the bones of the foot; and to those of the foot few fractures happen, and they do not occur there except as a result of improper positioning.
And if a fracture occurs in the bones of the foot and these bones are found to be elevated above each other, then the patient should rest their foot on prepared ground as follows.
And then place the foot on what is elevated by those bones, and then press them down, and bind them until they return to their proper places.
And then apply a plaster and place padding above it, and below the arch of the foot place a small board with two padded ends.
And so wrap that limb with bandages and other materials as you need, since you have laid it out in a descending structure.
And when three or four days have passed, you then loosen the bandage, and you will find the bones either aligned, or broken or dislocated, and the procedure in those cases is as you know.
But if some say they are broken, then repair them and align them as I have recommended to you in the treatment of the bones of the hand. And then prepare the broken finger with a splint according to the length of the finger, and make it slightly longer; and then place this limb on the table that I have recommended to you, and wrap it with proper bandaging.
But if what is broken consists of two or three or more bones, then you prepare a splint for each finger made in the appropriate quantity and wrapped in linen cloth, and fix the table at the lowest part of the limb, and let it stand on one end of the table so that the weight rests on the palm of the hand to ensure good retention.
And it is important for you not to forget that you should consider all the causes mentioned to you in other fractures and accidents that we have discussed; and prepare yourself for every accident with what is needed.
Chapter XVIII.
On the fracture of the chin bone of a woman and of the bone of the penis and the shaft of the penis in a man.
When the chin of a woman is broken, then make that seam in a downward direction, and then slightly incline it towards one side, and let it be held from behind.
Let the midwife fill the basin with cotton wool, and place it under her in the same manner as before; then let the woman be tilted forward slightly, and raise the child's buttocks gradually, for all of this should be done so that when she is facing away from the birth passage, as was desired, the broken bone can return to its proper position.
Then place poultices on his back, and when he wishes to urinate, move the cotton wool aside to facilitate it, and after urination reduce them and return those that were moved according to the method by which they were arranged at the beginning, and then return to the original poultice.
And do this for seven days or around that time, for he will be restored then.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and stitch onto it a tube of reed; then insert the entire bladder into his birth passage, and blow air through the tube vigorously until the bladder is inflated inside the birth passage, for the fracture will return to its proper position.
And then it should be filled with cotton and placed for several days as we have recommended, until he is healed.
And when the bone of the pubis in a man or woman is fractured, administer what we have recommended for the hip bone in its restoration and alignment.
And what is correct is not hidden in these nearby fractures that rarely occur, especially for one who has studied and used this book; for this fracture signifies to you in the major positions the straight path in its restoration and alignment, and thus you understand well.
In the penis of the man when it is grasped, take a piece of silk, and put the penis in that; and then press upon its wrapping of cloth, and let it be wrapped up, and then let it lie loose for three days or thereabouts, and it will heal.
Chapter XIX.
On the restoration of fractures of the bones when there is swelling.
It is convenient for him who has suffered a fracture with swelling, and especially if the bone is large like the thigh bone or the humerus, or similar to these, that you cauterize him immediately if the conditions for cauterization are apparent to you as we have said above.
And if the swelling discharges blood, then it is necessary that you treat for retention of breath in this way: powder over it burnt eggshell, unless some other cause presents itself to you.
And then begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not delay this unless an abscess occurs.
But if an abscess forms, then you should delay the restoration for nine days until the abscess subsides, and do not approach it on the third day or the fourth, for you will cause worse accidents.
But if the broken bone protrudes above the skin, then it is necessary that you reduce it and equalize it with your hands as easily and with little extension.
But if extension is not prepared for you and the reduction of it with your hands, then reduce it with your hands using this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, its length being seven or eight fingers, and its width according to the size of the swelling; and therefore it is necessary for the surgeon to have three or four of these, according to what may be needed for this in all kinds of fractures.
And it should be round, and should have little grooves so that it is not doubled over during compression upon that one at the time of the operation.
And it should have a sharp point at which there is a margin in its extreme parts; and the upper part of it should be thick, and from the middle to the sides it should be divided.
And this is the form of it:
And it is called in Greek "labrum voluntas atelarem," which means a small palette.
So that the end of it may be placed with the tip raised above the end of the prominent bone.
And press it in one turn so that the bone returns to its place, and make it even with some adjustment.
And then strive to align the ends of the fracture between them, and if the broken end is delicate and does not accept the instrument well, you should insert the end of that bone so that the instrument has access to it.
