But if you cannot reduce the bone completely as we have recommended, then insert it with what seems appropriate from among the saws that we have mentioned; or cut it with one of the saws in whatever way is possible for you.
And then smooth that which remains on the bone of roughness and small ridges.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient finds great pain in biting down, then you know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; and thus do what will be of great benefit to the patient.
When you have completed the restoration of the bone that you are performing, and it is time to soak a cloth in black styptic vinegar, especially if it is summer, and do not apply cerate or any other cause that contains oil on the wound, so that no putrefaction or corruption may occur there.
And also administer styptic strips at the time of your completion of the restoration of the bone, and leave the wound uncovered in this way so that you can incise any protrusions with scissors and use bandages according to the extent of the wound.
And be careful not to touch the wound with your hands on the fracture, for many times this has been done by careless surgeons, and it has caused death or a chronic illness in their patients, which is called gangrene. And if the swelling is slight, soft, and different from the swelling of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or a bad wound, and you fear some of the harmful accidents we have mentioned, and you feel pain in that area, then it is not necessary to apply styptic strips; rather, make a bandage with hard cloths instead of strips and apply it to the wound.
And after one day or two, when the wound begins to produce pus in it, then you remove the cloth that you had placed over it with vinegar, and afterwards apply herbs, including those from our custom that are used to treat wounds, such as tetrafarmakon and similar ones.
And it is necessary for you to loosen the bandage and examine the wound every morning and evening until it is consolidated and healed, and it should be prepared in such a way that putrefaction can run off from it downward with ease.
If after many days the swelling does not consolidate nor is it cut away from there by the hand, then you should know that here are signs of small abscesses because you must probe the swelling with a probe. And of those abscesses, those that are separated should be cut out and extracted; those that do not separate and cause pain when the limb is moved, you should incise according to what is possible for you.
And if the swelling turns into pus or becomes ulcerated, which is a sign of corruption and putrefaction, then you should proceed according to one of those methods that are suitable for treating it, as previously described in its chapter.
And concerning this matter in which I must speak and you should pay attention: when the bone is fractured, it protrudes above the limb like the bones of the wrist and elbow and similar to those of large limbs, and do not attempt traction or extraction because doing so might lead to death after four movements; instead, leave it inside as it will likely heal within about twenty days or thirty; and then treat the swelling if you see fit, and if not, leave it alone.
Chapter XX.
On the treatment of stiffness that occurs as a consequence of some fracture.
This stiffness often occurs as a consequence of the healing of a fracture, especially near the joints, and it distorts the shape of the limb; and to a great extent, it prevents the limb from performing its natural movements.
Then consider if the stiffness is recent and administer medicines that have astringent properties such as aloes, rosemary, myrrh, saffron, and similar ones; take any of these or all of them, and mix them with vinegar or egg white or fresh water, and apply this mixture over the stiffness on a piece of cloth, and bind it tightly with proper compression.
There is no release that you should not loosen it with words day by day; and then loose it and put on another, until the swelling has subsided; or lay a cloth upon that lead plate gradually, for lead has the property of reducing all that is inflamed in the limbs.
But if the swelling has already turned into a hard lump and it is necessary to remove it immediately due to its severity, then cut it at the part where pus is most abundant, and excise the superfluous and prominent parts; or reduce it with some instruments until it subsides, and take care of it until it heals.
Chapter XXI.
On the restoration of fractures when they are set and then the limb is moved gently not contrary to its nature.
When a bone fracture is set, and then the limb is moved gently and carefully, this is done for several reasons: one of which is excessive soaking of the bandages and wrapping them not as needed; or by too tight binding of the bandages preventing nourishment from reaching the limb; or by excessive embrocation; or by unnecessary movement at the wrong time; or due to a lack of blood in the patient's body and debility.
And the treatment for this is to give food to the patient and make their body soft so that blood multiplies in them; administer baths; and apply poultices and lint to it.
And then apply a poultice to the limb, so that the poultice draws nourishment to it, or continue embrocation with warm water, until nourishment flows and returns to its natural course.
Chapter XXII.
On the treatment of broken bones when they heal incorrectly and prohibit their operation from what is necessary.
When it happens that a member already restored near the healing of its curvature and prominence in the broken bone and nodulation, and this is due to the deformation of the shape of the member, yet the member is not prohibited from its natural operation, then there is no need for you to accept the word of one who believes that member to be newly broken.
But now it stands that false doctors and restorers have done this in our land; and this operation is a shameful act leading to great fear, the sign of which is fatal.
But if the limp and nodulation are recent, then it is necessary to bathe them in warm water containing softening herbs such as fern roots, linseed, and melilot, and similarly with those; and a soft plaster should be applied as well, prepared properly; or take mucilage of flax seed mixed with wheat bran or olive oil, and apply it; or take fatty figs and mash them with dove's milk, and similarly with the medicines mentioned for strengthening.
And in some cases, it is dissolved through frequent gentle friction, which should be done with the most humid substances, and movement of the member should be administered to all parts at all times.
But if the curvature has already advanced and is strong, having turned into bone and necessitating treatment with iron, then it is necessary to drain the pus above and dissolve the continuation of the bone, and carefully cut what is excessive from the nodulation of that part with a fine instrument, and facilitate this with diligence and study.
Chapter XXIII.
On Speaking of Dislocations.
Dislocation is when any of the joints loses its proper place, thereby prohibiting movement, and the figure of the limb is distorted, causing severe pain and great injury.
When a dislocation occurs, it is necessary to attempt its reduction as soon as possible and not delay in doing so, because if it is delayed, swelling will occur and the task of reducing the dislocation becomes much more difficult.
And for this reason, it is not advisable to move or keep the patient standing during the swelling period, as movement often causes spasms and intense pain.
But when this happens, it is necessary to prepare for the incision of the affected area, and then allow the swelling to subside; afterward, the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil, and then reduction should be attempted with ease.
And treat the entire limb with what will help it remember its proper place.
And thus I have organized the chapters on dislocation as they were preceded in fractures; starting from the upper part of the body to the lower part.
Chapter XXIV.
On the Treatment of Dislocation of the Lower Jaw.
When the two jaws are dislocated, it is not delayed; and the dislocation of both occurs in one of two ways: either they are slightly moved from their places and somewhat relaxed; or they are completely and entirely dislocated until they become relaxed at the joint where saliva flows, and the person cannot close their mouth or hold that position, and the tongue becomes wrapped within it.
When the dislocation of the joint is slight, it returns by itself in major positions due to little cause.
If the dislocation is complete and severe, it must be treated with prompt reduction, and should not be delayed at all.
And it is that the minister should have his finger of one hand enter into the mouth through the right side of the dislocation if the dislocation is on one side; or he should insert both fingers together if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers should remain outside equal to them; and he commands the patient to relax their jaws and allow them to move apart; and the physician then aligns the jaw and presses it until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated together, then apply evaporation with hot water and oil until the reduction can be performed gently on both sides, and do not delay the reduction of both sides at all, as we have said.
And when they return and are aligned, if the patient's mouth remains closed and is not relaxed, then place small pieces of cloth covered with wax on them, and make it from beeswax and oil softened; and attach it gently with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on their back, with their head positioned between two pillows, so that it does not turn to either the right or left side; and do not force them to chew for any reason; have their food be cut up finely until such time as when the pains are reduced and the jaws are well-aligned, then allow them to eat what they want.
With ease should it be administered, and let him not work on the opening of the mouth for eating and drinking and speaking until the jaw is healed and restored.
And if the reduction of both jaws when they are dislocated at any time and have not returned to their proper places becomes difficult, fevers and persistent sweats often occur, and the belly of the patient is easily distended, and vomiting pure bile frequently happens.
And when you see this, then you know that it is lost; and often he who suffers this dies within x days.
Chapter XXV.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and the extremity of the muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated from the chest inside by its continuation to the ribs; and sometimes it is dislocated outward, and this is apparent by sensation.
And the restoration of it is that the patient lies on his back and extends his arms. And then press the area with your palm with firm pressure, for it will return.
And then apply a plaster and powder to that area.
But the extremity of that which lies on the neck and is continuous with it does not become dislocated at any time; therefore, it must be reduced and bandaged as we have said and as you are prepared.
And then apply a plaster, powder, and bandage to that area, and instruct the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed.
And in this method of treatment the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter XXVI.
On the Reduction of Muscle Dislocation.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, one of which is when it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
The second is when it is dislocated above the muscle, and this happens quickly; and it is not dislocated behind by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated in front by the nerves.
It is most commonly dislocated outward below the titillium, and more so in those whose flesh is thin, it dislocates easily; but in those with thick muscles, it dislocates with difficulty.
It often happens to some people that a blow or fall causes the muscle to become inflamed and swollen, and it is subjected to this because it has already been dislocated.
Then you should be aware of this while you are initially treating them, and proceed with their treatment accordingly.
And the dislocation is known when it is below the titillium; thus, you can distinguish between a dislocated muscle and a normal one, as there is a clear difference between them; you will find a depression at the head of the muscle below the titillium upon touching the head of the muscle, and the injured person cannot move their arm to their ear, nor make any such movement with it.
If it is dislocated, you should place the feet above, and you will immediately find it evident, and it is not hidden.
And from this dislocation a quick reduction is made when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the minister raises the hand above to the upper parts; and then gently places the fingers below the joint, and elevates the articulation with strength to its place, and the minister raises the hand of the patient, and extends it there; and then gently lets it drop as it quickly returns.
But if it does not return as we have said, and after the initial time since the dislocation occurred, then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water and a linseed poultice mixed with items such as horse chestnut, fenugreek, and melilot in water should be administered.
And then the patient should be laid supine on their back and gently placed below the joint while waiting for wool to be prepared between levity and tightness. Then the physician places his thigh on the ball, and raises the head of the muscle with force, and pulls the patient's hand so that it is not hindered in the parts below, and another assistant holds the patient's head so that it is not hindered in the parts below, for it will quickly return to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this method: have a man present who is taller than the patient, and let him stand on that side, and insert his thigh below the joint of the patient and raise the joint above towards the upper parts while suspending the patient in the air, and another assistant pulls the patient's hand from the lower part of their abdomen. If the malady is chronic, now it must be that one who has another cause suspended, for so does he weigh down that which has been displaced, since the dislocation returns so soon.
And it is reduced in another manner: that a long pole be planted in the ground with its head round like a mortar pestle, neither thick nor thin, and placed near the patient; then place over the head of the pole strong cloths, and the patient stands according to the length of the pole, and stretch one hand beyond on one side; and extend the body on the other side with strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if the reduction of it is difficult due to all the causes we have mentioned, now use this treatment: take a pole whose length is two fathoms, and its width be four digits, and the thickness of it should be proportionate to the size of a finger according to this form:
And then place strong cloths over the round head so that the pole does not hurt the patient.
And then place the pole near the patient's finger, and stretch the hand or arm over the pole on one side, and tie the pole with assistants and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then place the arm on a step of a ladder according to its width, and stretch the hand to one side, and suspend the other body on the other side, for the joint returns quickly.
And when the instrument for it is completed according to each manner that is possible for you, then place a pad of wool under the finger of equal quantity; and then apply a plaster prepared with powder of myrtle and egg white over all the muscle, as mentioned above, and then bind tightly around the finger appropriately; let the bandage be wrapped around the plaster, and attach the hand to the neck, and leave it so, and do not disturb the hand for seven days.
Let the food of the sick person be moderate until the limb is strengthened, for it is easier to heal thus.
And let the food be given more around seven or five times and let one exercise a movement; and thus if it is stopped and not softened, now it is healed.
If the joint is dislocated due to moisture that occurs from it or for some other reason, then cautery should be administered three times in that area, as was said before in the chapter on cautery.
And if all this is done and after seven days the bandage is removed and the joint is not stable, and the plaster and compression are reapplied several times, but it remains unstable and the patient cannot raise that arm above certain points, then you will know that the nerves of it, which control the muscle at its end, have been severed or extended or softened, and understand that the joint will never be properly set in place.
Regarding the dislocation that occurs due to the feet and the thigh or the parts behind, the reduction is done with impulse and extension as much as necessary until it returns to its place.
And let the other compression be applied to it, and care for it until it is healed.
And if heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement occur during recovery, then the patient should use baths several times until that heaviness is alleviated and it returns to its original nature.
Chapter XXVII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the collarbone.
Know that the joint of the collarbone is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, but mainly forward and backward. And this dislocation does not hide from you as it appears below the sight or touch, depending on its figure; and when you adjust the dislocated bone, that is clearly declared to you, for the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm, nor can it touch its muscle.
And you should treat it and reduce the dislocation as soon as possible before it becomes infected, for if it reaches an infected state, reducing it will be difficult, and it will not heal completely; especially when it is in the backward position, for this is the worst of all types of dislocations, causing the most pain, and many people die because of it.
And the restoration of it, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the hand be extended by two fingers, and the arm be expanded, and the physician's hand should be on the collarbone below it, and he should push the joint with his fingers together, or with the palm of his hand until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in the forward direction, then it returns as he bends his hand with his other hand, until he strikes the collarbone with the palm of his hand.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer vigorous extension and strong force.
And it is that two assistants extend the arm, and hold it firmly by both ends so that it does not move due to the extension.
And after you have rolled up the arm, cover it completely with a long folded cloth or bandage.
And when the surgeon touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that this will help in the lubrication of the joint and facilitate movement; then forcefully push the joint inward until it returns to its place.
And after the reduction of the shoulder, it should be placed on a plaster cast that is stiff and secure with linen bandages, and it should be bound tightly but not too constrictively, and the arm should be suspended from the neck, and left like this for several days.
And then it should be loosened; and if the joint is formed in its proper place, now you may untie the bandage from it and leave it as is.
And if you see that the joint does not heal properly, then replace the plaster and bandage, and leave it like this for several days until it is healed; and afterwards, loosen it.
But if it happens that the patient experiences severe pain during the reduction of the joint and their disposition is in movement, then administer hydration through bathing and gentle friction, and compression, until they are comforted; or place something soothing on the joint, which is caused by inflammatory swelling; and afterwards bind it, and leave it on the bone day and night. And then remove that and place the patient in a bath; when they are dry, now you should rub the joint with moderate friction, and afterwards apply something soothing on it again, which is due to inflammatory swelling, once, and the second time, and the third time. take an instrument for probing, enter with it carefully.
And if you wish, place a piece of fresh cowhide over the moist and inflamed wound and bandage it; do this for several applications, as the dressing keeps the area moist and reduces inflammation.
Chapter XXVIII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the wrist, which is the joint between the arm and the hand.
When the wrist of the hand is dislocated, reducing this dislocation can often be relatively easy compared to other joints. However, it is important that reduction occurs quickly when the dislocation happens, before swelling or infection sets in.
To reduce the dislocation of the wrist, place the joint of the injured hand on a table and have an assistant extend the hand. The physician then places their hand or palm over the joint and gently presses it until it returns to its proper position.
However, it must be considered whether the palm is already displaced towards the back of the hand. If so, the injured person should place the back of their hand on a table in an extended position for reduction; if the dislocation is towards the front of the hand, then the hand should be positioned flat on the table, so that the physician's hand can press directly onto the joint.
And if it returns to its place immediately, that is good; but if not, then bandage it to allow for swelling and leave it as such; do not attempt this again, because otherwise, the injury will neither tolerate nor permit further reduction after several days.
However, the joint remains curved according to its tortuosity; and you do not show any reason to the patient, except that your hand is softened and cannot grasp any reason; now you know that the nerves are cut or torn, and now it does not arise there but rather must be strengthened with cautery near the site and thus confer some cause.
Then when the dressing is changed, now place on the bandage which we have recommended, and let it be left and strengthened for five days; and afterwards it should be loosened and left, and the hand should be exercised.
However, if the movement of it remains and some gravity occurs in it, now treat that with hot water and friction several times, until it is alleviated.
Chapter XXIX.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the said joints.
Sometimes the said joints are dislocated completely.
And when any of them is dislocated towards the thumb or towards the palm, now press the finger and push the dislocation with your fingers until it returns.
And then support the tip of the finger, and suspend that above the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
And then suspend that and extend it until it is properly set in its place on that day.
And when it is night, bind that according to the narration, and do not stop loosening it during the day and exercising with movement, and bind it at night; and do this for several days until it is strong.
If it appears to be such, if it is dislocated from the palm of the hand, and bind that part in that manner and do with this the first operation, until it is healed.
And similarly do with this when it is dislocated from other parts.
Chapter XXX.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the spinal vertebrae.
When one of the spinal vertebrae or neck vertebrae is completely dislocated and the bones move out of their places, there is no treatment at that moment, for death is feared for the patient.
The sign of this is that the movement of the patient occurs without their will, and they cannot control it; often some parts of them or one or two arms or one or two legs are weakened; or one vertebra has moved from its place, as sometimes it moves and at other times returns to its proper position in four directions.
When it is already displaced to the rear parts, it is called a kyphosis.
However, the treatment for this is to determine whether the kyphosis is due to weakness, because in that case there is no cure and it will not heal completely.
However, in cases where it occurs due to falling or impact or similar causes, the Ancients described the methods of treatment at length in their discourse, which I will now summarize concisely; for what little is omitted does not detract from its clarity, given my proximity to understanding and my exposition, in which I have presented this and formulated it from various sources different from those used by others who might demonstrate or explain it.
And of those, it is not cured except that which happens in the two properly as I mention.
And it is that the patient be extended on his back on a bed equally near to a wall, and be extended below with the bedding moist so that the feet of the patient are not dried out.
And nearby a straight post should be placed, set in the ground at the end of the bed near the head of it, and another post around the feet of it at the other end of the bed.
And the minister should hold the post, and it should not be fixed in a pit; and another minister should hold the other according to that form.
And nearby wrap around the head of the patient and below the shoulders tie the linen firmly; and extend the end of the bandage to the post which is behind the head of him, and bind that to it; and then stretch the other bandage over the hips of him with his knees, and behind the buttocks of him; and then adjust all the bandages and bind those to the other post which is behind the feet of him.
And then the minister should extend the bandage to the side of the post, and do not move the two posts and the bandages from their places in which they are fixed, but rather ensure they are not too tight as we have said.
And the physician should place his palms on the spine with strength until it returns, or place upon that a table, and then adjust the patient's feet on the table until it returns.
If the mast does not return in this manner, take a table whose width or length is about three arms' lengths, and dig in the wall near the patient a place where the end of the table can fit. And place the middle of the table on the spine, and the physician places his feet on the other end, and gently pulls until the spine is compressed and returns to its proper place.
And if you want, open again the one that is wrapped around the hand; and this is how you place the patient's head between the ends of two poles, whose length should be one arm, and behind his head another pair of poles in the same manner; and the distance between each pole should be a palm, and they are made such that there is a groove through which to pull, and each end is firmly fixed to the ground so it does not move.
And there should be a round pole through which the rope runs, and it should be wrapped around the two grooves of the two poles.
And at the end of it there is a groove in which the pole is formed, whose length is one palm where it is wrapped; and on the other two poles there is something similar.
And then pull the ropes that you have tied to the patient's feet at the groove behind his head, and the ropes that you have tied to the patient’s waist at the rope behind his feet.
And then behind each rope should be a helper who turns the rope with their hand, and the physician will guide the spine as we have said.
And after the spine is returned to its place and the area is leveled, it is necessary to apply an excruciating plaster mixed with egg whites, and then cover it with wool; and afterwards place over the plaster a strip of wood whose width is three fingers or around that size, and whose length should match the extent of the inflammation, and this should be positioned at the part of the spine.
And afterwards, wrap it with the bandage we have mentioned, and use a diet of light nourishment until he is healed.
And if there is any inflammation in the area at the end of the healing process, it is necessary to administer medicines that soften and soothe, along with the prolonged application of the board we have recommended; and a lead plate is also applied to that location.
And sometimes inflammation occurs at the back of the lower spine due to that dislocation; and this bone is one where it should not be anticipated to use such medicine, for it could easily lead to death.
Chapter XXXI.
On the Treatment of a Dislocated Hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the muscle attachment are susceptible only to dislocation, and do not suffer what happens to other joints with minor movement and concavity.
And the joint of the hip can be dislocated in four ways: either inward, outward, forward, or backward.
And it is mostly displaced towards the interior parts, and a little toward the front or back parts.
The sign of the dislocation towards the interior parts is that when you adjust the leg of the healthy person next to the injured one, it appears longer and its heel more prominent than the injured leg. The injured person cannot bend their knee towards their chin, as the place where this can happen has become swollen due to cauterization for apostema, so the head of the bone has already reached here.
The sign of the one to whom dislocation occurs towards the outer parts is that the symptoms are opposite to those mentioned earlier.
The sign of the one to whom dislocation occurs towards the front is that when the leg is raised according to its full length, it does not bend due to pain in the heel, and if he attempts to walk, he cannot do so forward; and his urine is retained and his throat becomes swollen, and he walks on tiptoe.
The sign of the one to whom dislocation occurs towards the rear is that he cannot raise his heel, nor can he bend forward as far as he bends his throat, and his leg is shorter than the other, and his throat is softened, and the head of the bone is behind that place.
However, the reduction of these types of dislocation is that you consider whether the dislocation is old and ancient. If it has already become accustomed to being in that position, you administer reduction according to its disposition, but at this point it cannot be completely cured; therefore, there should not be too much expectation for a full recovery.
In the case where the dislocation is recent, and it is one of the four types of dislocation, place your hand so that you can return the joint to its proper position by extending it inwards and outwards, and move it from right to left, for it easily returns and does not require other treatment.
If it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare a strong support and extend the leg from below the part that is behind, or above its joints, or apply a bandage over the upper thigh; and another person should extend the hand of the patient from above so as to place their hands behind the joints; and immediately fasten a lion-shaped bandage around the root of the joint, and have another person hold the end of the bandage.
And this extension of the arm or of the part inside towards the shoulder blade, or of the part behind towards the back.
And this extension should be done all at once while the patient is raised off the ground and remains hanging.
And this type of extension is common to all four types.
And thus if the dislocation returns as we have said, it is good; and if not, then for every type what I say about its specific treatment is necessary.
In the reduction of the shoulder when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is towards the inside, the patient should be laid on their side; then place bandages around the root of the joint at the location between the head of the joint and the place behind the shoulder blade; then extend the bandage from above the armpit to the farthest part of the shoulder blade.
Let another strong minister take the limbs of the patient and firmly grasp the base of the affected joint and extend it forcefully to its parts outward by strong extension, for that returns to its place.
And this species is the quickest to cure among those to which this member is reduced compared to the others.
But if it is excused upon you and you do not obey the training in this species of cure entirely, then now it is necessary to lift both sides of the patient together with a strong, quick bandage over both heels and both toes.
And let the length of each one be the quantity of four fingers, and let the affected side be extended more than the other by a quantity of two fingers.
And let the patient be suspended above the top of the post, and hold him by his hand; and let the distance from him to the ground be the length of two arms.
And then command a strong young man to pull the head of the joint, and let another young man hold the patient, while the other young man pulls the joint with great strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
But in the proper reduction, when it is dislocated to the outer part, let the patient lie on his back as we have recommended for lying down comfortably; and let the bandage be tightly applied to the affected side properly and over his feet.
And then two posts should be placed, one at his feet and the other at his head; and then place closely adjusted posts on each side of him laid upon which are wrapped damp cloths, so that the patient is not harmed by the post being between his joints, to prevent the extension downward from being pulled.
Let each attendant extend his part, and the surgeon with his skill completes the reduction according to art.
And when the dislocation is to the front parts, then it is necessary that the limb be extended quickly, and according to this method of narration on the back; and the surgeon places the palm of his right hand on the affected joint, and then compresses it with the other hand, and in this compression extends it from the lower part towards the front part.
And if the dislocation is to the rear parts, then it is not necessary for the patient to be extended towards the lower part, but rather elevated above the ground; however, it should be placed on a hard surface, as it would be similarly necessary if the hip were dislocated outward, according to what we have said about the positioning of the body on two boards; and it is upon his back with the limbs extended, as we have said, for healing.
And it is necessary to apply compression to the table at the location of the joint.
Thus, when the reduction is complete as it should be — and the sign of the hip reduction is not hidden from you, which is that you extend both limbs of the patient, and when you see them equal and the patient can move one leg without difficulty, then you know that the limb has returned as it should — and then you adjust two things and apply bandages so that the hip is not injured in any part; and adhere to having the patient rest for three or four days.
Then loosen the bandage and the dressing, and compare one side with the other.
Now, if you see those marks on the garment, now you know that the dislocation has already been reduced, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any cause for softening, now you return and apply a plaster to it, and wrap it according to your first structure, and let it be for three days.
Then loosen that [bandage], and have the patient delay in walking on each [limb] for several days, until they are comfortable.
Chapter XXXII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the knee.
The knee can be dislocated in three ways: it can be dislocated outward, inward, and backward; it cannot be dislocated forward.
And the sign of dislocation of it is that you command the patient to apply their hand to the chest; now you know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all ways of it is that you make the patient lie straight, so they can extend their leg as much as possible; adhere your hand to the side of it, and place your feet between the patient’s feet; then make adhere the knee with your palms, and hold those between your hands on the knee firmly, and another assistant extends the patient's foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of reduction of it is that the patient’s hand adheres to the chest effortlessly.
Apply the plaster to it and make the cup adhere to the wound, then bind those with a bandage for three days or four, and then untie those.
And do not use the affected area for walking for several days until it is healed and comfortable.
But if the reduction of the thigh to the hip is unnecessary, now administer extensive treatment with the methods previously described in the healing of the hip.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the heel.
The heel sometimes is displaced by slight movement, and sometimes it is dislocated completely; the dislocation of it is done either towards the interior parts or towards the exterior parts.
And the sign of the dislocation of it is that you see the heel swollen towards the part to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment for the slight displacement of it is easy reduction, and it should be extended with ease and gradually returned to its place until it heals.
But in the treatment of it when it is completely dislocated, ensure that the affected area is properly treated, and let a skilled and strong practitioner handle it from behind both sides at the midpoint of it.
Then place your right foot on the highest part, and your left hand below the foot at the location of the heel.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then use your left hand twice, and then extend it with your left hand; and press the toes of the foot with your right hand around the cup while working.
Here are two veins as we have counted them.
And then press the feet of the patient to the third vein, and you will find it at the knot.
Therefore, if it returns in one vein or in two according to this method, and if the foot heals properly, it is good; and otherwise, at that time repeat the operation, for it will return.
But if you are prohibited from reducing the limb as we have recommended, then lay the patient on their back on the ground, and place a board firmly on the ground under the affected area, ensuring it fits between the parts of the limb, and wrap it with cloth so that the limb does not slip.
And then the attendant should hold the patient's limbs, and another attendant should extend the affected limb with their hands or with straps around the neck of the foot; and then each attendant should pull against the extension according to their position, and the board should be extended between the parts of the limb, holding it in place so that the body does not be pulled away from below during its extension.
And then the physician should heal the dislocation with his hands, and another attendant should hold the healthy limb below, as the dislocation will return with ease.
And when the dislocation returns and you confirm the successful reduction of the limb, now apply a plaster and bind it firmly with bandages, and secure the foot with straps to the board.
And you must be careful of the nerves that are behind the heel, so that the bandage over them is not too tight and does not injure them severely.
Let it remain like that for two days or for three; and if the dressing has softened, remove it.
Then loosen that on the third day or on the fourth, and forbid the patient from moving for forty days. But if he is forced to move before that time, there is no guarantee that the dislocation will not be dissolved and corrupted, and then it will not respond to treatment.
And if it happens that the abscess is cauterized, then you must administer according to the treatment described in our narration, in another place for curing and applying ointments, until it heals.
Chapter XXXIIII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the said foot joints.
It is necessary that the one who is dislocated rest with some extension without work; and what is not difficult is, in fact, easy.
But if the dislocation is in some joints of the two feet, then you must have the patient lie on a level surface of the ground or on a table, and let them be positioned as if they were standing.
Then stand on one foot and place your foot over the joint that needs correction, and then step on it firmly until it returns to its proper position, and you see that it is straightened and no longer protrudes.
Then place the foot on a board that covers the entire foot, which has two ends, and then bind it with proper and firm bandaging for three days; then loosen it and have the patient rest from walking for several days until it is healed and recovery is assured.
Chapter XXXV.
Concerning the types of dislocations that occur due to plague or fracture or any such circumstances.
When any such cause occurs, and you proceed with the treatment of it and the restoration of it, often death results.
And because of this, one should not undertake the treatment of it presumptuously, but only someone who is wise in the art, experienced over time, merciful and pious; who uses at the beginning medicines that soften tumors slightly, and leaves the patient to divine judgment unless there appears some hope for salvation from death or relief from illness. Then you should strive to reduce it as soon as possible at the beginning of the cause, before it becomes a hard tumor.
And then if the limb returns as we wish, immediately administer a regimen that softens the hard tumor and treats the wound with what is appropriate from among the dry applications.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and occurs in the bones that are separated, then you should proceed with the treatment of them as we have said concerning simple illnesses, treating the locations where they have occurred.
And guard and avoid, according to your virtue, the use of instruments in ways that cause fear as I have previously advised you; for this is better for you to serve your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more salutary to your true body, which you will see in your time. Thus finely speaks Albucasis perfectly of which God be blessed. Amen.
